Marriage of a Muslim Woman to a Non-Muslim: Absolute Prohibition and Effect of Apostasy
Stating ruling on marriage with non-Muslim and apostasy's effect

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on marriage between a Muslim woman and a non-Muslim, and explain the effect of apostasy on an existing marriage.

Core Idea
A Muslim woman can never marry a non-Muslim; apostasy does not dissolve the marriage but makes her forbidden until repentance.

This lesson clarifies the absolute prohibition of a Muslim woman marrying any non-Muslim, and the surprising Hanafi ruling that apostasy does not automatically dissolve a marriage. Understanding this distinction is crucial for fatwa writing in cases of interfaith marriage and apostasy. The passage also notes that a Muslim man may only marry a Kitabiyya woman; all other non-Muslim women are forbidden.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Recognize that the passage addresses two scenarios: (1) marriage of a Muslim woman to any non-Muslim, and (2) marriage of a Muslim man to a non-Muslim woman who is not Kitabiyya. Both are ruled absolutely haram and invalid (batil).
2
Note the exception for Kitabiyya
For a Muslim man, marriage to a Kitabiyya (Jewish or Christian woman) is permitted. No such exception exists for a Muslim woman.
Kitabiyya: woman from the People of the Book.
3
Understand the effect of apostasy on an existing marriage
If a married Muslim woman apostatizes, the marriage is not dissolved (faskh). However, she becomes haram (forbidden) to her husband until she repents and renews the marriage (tajdid al-nikah).
This is the Hanafi position; other schools differ.
4
Apply the ruling to a scenario
Given a case of a Muslim woman who converts to Christianity, state: her marriage remains valid but she is haram for her husband until she returns to Islam. Also state that no new marriage with a non-Muslim is possible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسلمان عورت کا کافر سے نکاح (یعنی عورت کے مرتد ہونے سے ) واضح رہے کہ اس کا روائی سے شرعا نکاح فسخ نہیں ہوتا اگرچہ تجدیر اسلام اور تجدید نکاح سے قبل موجودہ شوہر کے لیے ہمیشہ ہی وغیرہ حرام ہو جاتی ہے اس مسئلہ کو اس رسالہ کا قیمہ میں یہ بھی وضاحت سے بیان کیا گیا ہے کہ مسلمان عورت کا نکاح کسی کافر سے کرنا ہرگز جائزہ نہیں ہے قطعاً حرام ہے اور اسی طرح کتابیہ عورت کے علاوہ کسی کافر عورت سے مسلمان مرد کا نکاح بھی بالکل باطل اور قطعی حرام ہے ۔
Muslim woman's marriage to a non-Muslim (i.e., due to the woman's apostasy). It is clear that from the narration, legally the marriage is not dissolved, although after [tajdeer?] Islam and before renewing the marriage, she becomes permanently forbidden to the existing husband, etc. This issue has also been explained clearly in the [qeema?] of this treatise that marrying a Muslim woman to any non-Muslim is absolutely impermissible, definitely forbidden. Similarly, except for a Kitabiyya woman, marriage of a Muslim man to any non-Muslim woman is absolutely void and definitely forbidden.
Source pages p. 34

Commentary
This passage addresses the ruling on marriage when a Muslim woman apostatizes. According to the Hanafi school, apostasy does not dissolve the marriage immediately; rather, the woman becomes forbidden to her husband until she repents and renews the marriage. However, the passage emphatically states that a Muslim woman cannot marry a non-Muslim under any circumstances; such a marriage is absolutely forbidden and invalid. Similarly, a Muslim man is only permitted to marry a Muslim woman or a Kitabiyya (Jewish/Christian) woman; any other non-Muslim woman is forbidden. This is a settled ruling in the Hanafi madhhab.

Key Terms
نکاح (nikah): Marriage contract in Islamic law
فسخ (faskh): Dissolution of marriage, annulment
کافر (kafir): Non-Muslim, specifically one who is not a Kitabi
کتابیہ (kitabiyya): Woman from the People of the Book (Jew or Christian)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what happens if a Muslim woman apostatizes while married?
A) The marriage is immediately dissolved.
B) The marriage is not dissolved, but she becomes forbidden to her husband until she repents.
C) The marriage becomes void automatically.
D) She must be divorced immediately.

Key Takeaway
A Muslim woman's marriage to a non-Muslim is absolutely prohibited and invalid; apostasy does not dissolve the marriage but makes her forbidden until repentance.

Knowledge Requirement for Shariah Committee Members
Ruling on knowledge as condition for Shariah committee validity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi-influenced rule that a Shariah committee's decision is invalid if its members lack knowledge and do not consult qualified scholars, and construct the practical hierarchy for fatwa issuance.

Core Idea
A Shariah committee without knowledge is invalid, even if its ruling accidentally matches the truth.

This passage establishes a crucial rule for modern Shariah committees: knowledge is not optional but a condition for validity. The author, following the Maliki school on this point, outlines a hierarchy of acceptable scenarios, from a fully qualified panel to the necessity of consulting muftis. Understanding this rule is essential for anyone serving on or evaluating the rulings of such committees.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal issue
Determine what qualification is required for arbitrators in Shariah committees, especially for cases like faskh nikah.
The passage emphasizes that laypeople's arbitration has no weight.
2
Understand the three-tier hierarchy
First ideal: all members are ahl ilm. Second: at least one expert scholar (mu'amalah fahm 'alim) is included and consulted. Third: if that is impossible, the case must be referred to muftis and decided by their fatwa.
The hierarchy shows a decreasing level of direct qualification.
3
Analyze the consequence of non-compliance
If none of these conditions are met, the committee's decision is null and void, even if it coincidentally matches the correct Shariah ruling.
This is a strict rule: correctness does not cure lack of authority.
4
Examine the Maliki proof
The author cites Mukhtasar Khalil stating that the ruling of an ignorant person is rejected even if he consults, and commentary by Allamah al-Duwayyar explains why even knowledge of the ruling may be insufficient for proper execution.
The proof is from Maliki fiqh, adapted here for the committee context.
5
Connect to Hanafi iftaa practice
This rule aligns with the Hanafi requirement that a mufti must be qualified; the committee's decision must be based on authoritative fatwa.
The fatwa hierarchy becomes crucial when the committee itself is not composed of scholars.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
شرعی کمیٹی میں اہل علم افراد کی شرکت اگر فسخ نکاح وغیرہ فیصلہ پنچائیت یعنی شرعی کمیٹی کے سپرد کر لیا جائے تو چونکہ عوام کی پنچائیت کا کچھ اعتبار نہیں ہے نہ معلوم کس جگہ شرعی احکام کی خلاف ورزی کریں اس وجہ سے سب سے پہلے تو یہ چاہیئے کہ پنچائیت یعنی شرعی کمیٹی کے تمام ممبران اہل علم ہوں اور اگر ایسا نہ ہو سکے کہ تمام ممبران اہل علم نہ سکیں تو کم سے کم ایک معاملہ فہم عالم کو پنچائیت یعنی شرعی کمیٹی میں اس طریقہ سے شامل کر لیا جائے کہ شروع سے آخیر تک جو کچھ بھی کریں اُن سے دریافت کر کے کریں۔ اور اگر یہ بھی ممکن نہ ہو تو پنچائیت (یعنی شرعی کمیٹی کا فیصلہ) نافذ اور معتبر ہونے کی کوئی صورت نہیں ہے کہ معاملہ کی مکمل رو نداد دکھلا کر دینی فسخ نکاح وغیرہ کے مقدمہ کی کاروائی کی پوری فائل دکھلا کر ہر ایک جنس کے شرعی حکم کو معاملہ فہیم محقق علماء کرام (یا مفتیان کرام) سے دریافت کر کے ان کے فتویٰ کے موافق فیصلہ کیا جائے اگر ایسا نہ کیا گیا بلکہ عوام نے بعض ایسی باتوں سے فیصلہ کر دیا تو وہ حکم نافذ نہ ہوگا۔ اگر چہ اتفاقاً فیصلہ صحیح بھی ہوگیا ہو جیسا کہ فقہاء مالکیہ نے اس کی وضاحت فرمائی ہے۔ جیسا کہ مختصر خلیل نامی کتاب میں ہے انہوں نے کہا ہے: وينبذ حكم جائر وجاهل لو يشاور وإلا تعقب ومعنى غير الجور وقال شارعه العلامة الدويدار تحت قوله لو يشاور اى العلماء ولو وافق الحق، والى ان قال ولا يعقب مع المشاورة وإن عرف الحكم فقد لا يعرف إيقاعه لأنه يحتاج لزيادة نظر فى البينة وغيرها من أحوال المتداعين إذ القضاء ضايقة دقيقة لا يهتدى إليه كل الناس قلنا ونظيره على قول بعض من صلى بغير التقوى أن صلوته لا تقع
Inclusion of knowledgeable persons in the Shariah committee: If the decision (of faskh nikah etc.) is entrusted to the panchayat, i.e., the Shariah committee, then since the panchayat of laypeople has no weight—it is not known where they will violate Shariah rulings—therefore, first of all it is necessary that all members of the panchayat (Shariah committee) be knowledgeable. And if that is not possible, i.e., all members cannot be knowledgeable, then at least one expert scholar should be included in the panchayat (Shariah committee) in such a way that whatever they do from beginning to end, they do it after consulting him. And if even this is not possible, then there is no way for the panchayat's decision to be enforceable and valid. Rather, the complete narration of the case must be presented—by showing the entire file of the proceedings of the religious faskh nikah case—and the Shariah ruling on each matter must be obtained from expert investigating scholars (or muftis), and the decision should be made in accordance with their fatwa. If this is not done, and laypeople decide based on some such things, then that ruling will not be enforceable, even if by chance the decision turned out to be correct, as the Maliki jurists have explained. As it is in the book Mukhtasar Khalil: they said: 'The ruling of a tyrant and an ignorant person is rejected even if he consults, otherwise it is overturned.' The meaning of 'ghayr al-jawr' (non-tyranny). The commentator, Allamah al-Duwayyar, said under his statement 'if he consults': i.e., the scholars, even if it coincides with the truth. And he further said: 'It is not overturned with consultation, even if he knows the ruling, because he may not know how to execute it, as it requires additional scrutiny of evidence and other circumstances of the litigants. Indeed, judgment is a precise matter not every person can guide to.' I said: its parallel is the statement of some: 'If one prays without taqwa, his prayer is not valid.'
Source pages p. 74

Commentary
This passage addresses the necessary qualification of knowledge for members of a Shariah committee, again in the context of following the Maliki school for matters like annulment of marriage. The author begins by stating that laypeople's arbitration has no standing because they are liable to violate Shariah rulings unknowingly. Therefore, the ideal is that all members of the committee be 'ahl ilm (knowledgeable). Failing that, at least one expert scholar (mu'amalah fahm 'alim) must be included, and all actions must be done after consulting him. If even that is impossible, then the committee's decision cannot be valid on its own. Instead, the entire case file must be presented to qualified muftis or scholars, and the decision must conform to their fatwa. If laypeople decide based on their own opinions, the ruling is null and void, even if it coincidentally matches the correct Shariah ruling. This is supported by a quotation from Mukhtasar Khalil, a key Maliki text, stating that the ruling of an ignorant person is rejected even if he consults, unless he consults scholars. Even then, he may know the ruling but not its proper application. The passage thus emphasizes that knowledge is essential for the validity of arbitration.

Key Terms
اہل علم (ahl ʿilm): Scholars who possess knowledge of Shariah rulings, particularly in the field being adjudicated.
مختصر خلیل (Mukhtaṣar Khalīl): A concise manual of Maliki fiqh by Khalil ibn Ishaq al-Jundi, widely used in Maliki legal education.
معاملہ فہم عالم (muʿāmalah fahm ʿālim): A scholar expert in transactional matters (mu'amalat) and legal cases.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if a Shariah committee does not include any knowledgeable person and does not consult scholars, what is the status of its decision?
A) It is valid if it is correct.
B) It is invalid and not enforceable.
C) It is valid but sinful.
D) It is valid only if all members agree.

Key Takeaway
Knowledge is a condition for the validity of a Shariah committee's ruling; consultation with scholars or muftis can substitute, but a layperson's decision is null even if it happens to be correct.

Defining the 'Innin in Hanafi Law
Quoting Fatawa Alamgiri to define 'innin.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define 'innin according to Fatawa Alamgiri, explain its relative scope, and identify the legal consequences.

Core Idea
An 'innin is defined relative to his inability to reach a specific woman, not absolute impotence.

This lesson examines the precise definition of 'innin (impotent man) from the authoritative Hanafi fatwa compilation, Fatawa Alamgiri. The definition is not absolute—it depends on the particular woman and the cause of inability. Understanding this nuance is crucial for determining the wife's right to faskh (judicial annulment).

Guided Path
1
Identify the key term and its basic definition
Locate the Arabic definition in the passage: al-'innin huwa alladhi la yasilu ila al-nisa' ma'a qiyam al-alah. Extract the core elements: he has a functioning organ but cannot engage in intercourse.
2
Analyze the relative condition
Note that the definition includes the phrase 'fa-in kana yasilu ila al-shayyib duna al-abkar' indicating that a man may be 'innin for virgins but not for previously married women. The disability is evaluated per woman, not universally.
This relative aspect is a distinctive feature of Hanafi fiqh.
3
Enumerate the causes
The text lists reasons for inability: 'arad (a defect), du'f fi khalqih (weakness), kirasatih (reluctance), or sihr (sorcery). Identify each and understand that the cause does not change the definition.
4
Apply the definition to a scenario
Given a specific wife, determine if the husband is 'innin in her regard. For example, if he can have intercourse with non-virgins but not with his virgin wife, he is 'innin for her.
This triggers her right to seek faskh.
5
Recognize implications for faskh
Understanding the relative definition is essential for the mufti: the wife's right to annulment depends on her husband's inability relative to her, not others.
The passage sets the foundation for subsequent rulings on procedure and mahr.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
نامر شخص کی بیوی کا طریقہ و کاروانی (1) شریعت کی اصطلاح میں عین (یعنی نامرد) کسی کو کہتے ہیں ؟ (۲) عنین (نامرد) کی بیوی کو نکاح کے فسح کا اختیار دیا جائے گا یا نہیں ؟ اگر اختیار دیا جائے تو اُس کی کیا صورت ہو گی ؟ اور اس کے لیے کیا شرائط ہیں ؟ تفریق (علیحدگی کے بعد منین (نام شخص) پر پورا مہر واجب ہوگا یا آدھا ؟ نیز عورت پر عدت لازم ہوگی یا نہیں ؟ الجواب (ا) في الباب الثاني عشر من طلاق العالمكيرية العنين هو الذى لا يصل إلى النساء مع قيام الالة فان كان يصل الى الشيب دون الابكار او الى بعض النساء دون البعض وذالك عرض به او نضعف في خلقه او بكيرستنه او سحر فهو عنين في حق من لا يصل اليهما كذا فى النهايه عا لمگیره منها ج ۲ قنادی عالمگیری کے بارہویں باب میں ہے کہ عین دینی عنین کی تعریف نامرد) اصطلاح شریعت میں وہ شخص ہے جو کہ عضو مخصوص ہونے کے باوجود عورت سے ہمبستری (جماع) پر قادر نہ ہو سکتا ہو اگر کوئی شخص کنواری لڑکی سے جماع پر تو قادر نہ ہو لیکن بکارت زائل شدہ عورت ے جو نفس کا عضو مخصوص کٹ گیا جو یا بالکل موجود نہ ہو اس کا حکم آگے مذکور ہے
Procedure and action regarding the wife of an impotent person. (1) In the terminology of Shari'ah, who is called 'inn (i.e. impotent)? (2) Should the wife of an 'innin (impotent) be given the option of faskh (annulment) of the marriage or not? If given, what is its procedure and what are its conditions? After separation, is the full mahr obligatory on the 'innin or half? Also, is iddah obligatory on the woman or not? Answer: (a) In the twelfth chapter of Talaq in al-Fatawa al-'Alamgiriyya: 'The 'innin is he who cannot reach women despite the existence of the organ. So if he can reach older women but not virgins, or some women but not others, due to a defect [؟] or weakness in his constitution, or due to his reluctance, or due to sorcery, then he is an 'innin with respect to those he cannot reach.' Similarly in al-Nihayah. Alamgiriyya, vol. 2. (The translation of the above:) According to Fatawa Alamgiri, in its twelfth chapter, the definition of 'innin (impotent) in shari'ah terminology is: a person who, despite having the specific organ, is unable to have intercourse with a woman. If a person is unable to have intercourse with a virgin but is able with a non-virgin, or [the organ is] cut off or altogether absent, its ruling is mentioned later.
Source pages p. 77

Commentary
This passage begins a new topic: the rulings pertaining to an impotent husband ('innin). The text first lists a series of questions in Urdu: the definition of 'innin, whether the wife has the right to annul the marriage (faskh), the procedure and conditions, the mahr obligation after separation, and the iddah. Then it provides the answer from Fatawa Alamgiri (the Hanafi fatwa compilation). The definition states that an 'innin is a man who cannot have intercourse with women despite having a functioning organ. Importantly, the definition is relative: if he can have intercourse with some women (e.g., older women) but not others (e.g., virgins), or with some but not all due to a physical defect, weakness, psychological reluctance, or sorcery, then he is considered an 'innin only with respect to those women he cannot reach. The Urdu commentary repeats this definition. The text notes that if a man cannot have intercourse with a virgin but can with a non-virgin, or if his organ is cut off or absent, the ruling for that case is mentioned later (not included in this passage). This lesson focuses solely on the definition.

Key Terms
عنين ('innin): A man who cannot have sexual intercourse with women despite having a functioning organ, due to a physical or psychological impediment.
فسخ (faskh): Annulment of the marriage contract by judicial decree at the request of the wife due to the husband's impotence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the definition from Fatawa Alamgiri, when is a man considered an 'innin?
A) When he cannot have intercourse with any woman at all
B) When he cannot have intercourse with his wife due to a permanent physical defect, even if he can have intercourse with other women
C) When he cannot reach the women he is with, despite having a functioning organ, due to reasons like weakness or sorcery
D) Only when his organ is completely absent

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi definition of 'innin is relative to each wife: a man is 'innin only for those women he cannot reach, even if he can reach others.

Definition and Procedure of 'Unnan (Impotence) in Hanafi Law
Rasm al-mufti: Legal definition and procedure for 'unin.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi legal definition of 'unin (impotence) and identify the procedure for a wife to obtain judicial separation (tafriq), including the waiting period and the dispute between lunar and solar years.

Core Idea
An 'unin man is defined relative to his wife: inability to consummate due to illness, weakness, old age, or magic.

This lesson unpacks the Hanafi ruling on impotence ('unin). We will define the term precisely and examine the legal procedure the wife must follow to seek separation. The passage also highlights a key difference among Hanafi scholars regarding the length of the waiting period—lunar versus solar year—and explains which opinion is relied upon for fatwa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition of 'unin
Read the definition given in the passage. Note that it is relative: a man may be 'unin for one wife but not another.
The key is relative inability due to specific causes (illness, magic, etc.).
2
List the conditions for the wife to claim tafriq
The wife must raise the case to a judge. The husband is asked and given a one-year respite. If he admits inability, the year starts.
The wife's claim of virginity may be verified by female witnesses.
3
Understand the judge's procedure
The judge asks the husband; if he denies, his oath is accepted unless the wife proves virginity. If he cannot swear, the year is ordered.
After the year, the wife can return to court if still not consummated.
4
Analyze the dispute between lunar and solar year
There is a difference: the apparent narration (Zahir al-Riwayah) says lunar year, but many later scholars prefer solar year for precaution.
Al-Sarakhsi and others argued solar year is more cautious.
5
Determine the mufta bihi position
The author notes that the solar year is the chosen view (mukhtar) and upon it is the fatwa.
Thus, in practice, the waiting period is a solar year.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
سے جماع پر قادر ہو ۔ (۲) یا بعض عورت سے ہمبستری پر قادر ہو لیکن دوسری عورت سے ہمبستری پر قادر نہ ہو اور یہ کسی بیماری ، یا کمزوری یا عمر رسیدہ ہونے کی وجہ سے ہو یا جادو وغیرہ کی وجہ سے ہو تو ایسا شخص جس عورت سے ہمبستری پر قادر نہ ہو اس کے حق میں عنین (نامرد) شمار ہوگا ۔ فتاوی عالمگیری مثل ج ۲ - عنین کی فقہی تعریف کی وضاحت مذکورہ بالا عبارت سے ثابت ہوا کہ حضرات فقہاء کرام کی اصطلاح میں عنین (نامرد) اُس شخص کو کہتے ہیں کہ جو کہ عضو مخصوص ہونے کے باوجود عورت سے جماع (ہمبستری) پر قدرت نہ رکھتا ہو، خواہ اس کی یہ کیفیت کسی بیماری کی وجہ سے پیدا ہوئی ہو یا کمزوری کی وجہ سے یا بڑھاپے کی وجہ سے پیدا ہوئی ہو یا اس وجہ سے پیدا ہوئی ہو کہ کسی نے اُس پر جادو کر دیا ہو اور اگر کوئی ایسا ہو کہ بعض عورتوں سے تو ہمبستری کرنے پر قادر ہے اور بعض پر نہیں تو جس سے ہمبستری پر قدرت نہیں اُس کے حق میں یہ شخص عنین (نامرد) سمجھا جائے گا۔ (۲) عنین (نامرد) کی بیوی (اگر طلاق لینا چاہے تو اس کو مندرجہ ذیل شرائط کے ساتھ تفریق یعنی علیحدگی اختیار کرنے کا شرعا حق حاصل ہے۔ (۳) الف - He is able to have intercourse. (2) Or if he is able to have intercourse with some women but not with another woman, and this is due to illness, weakness, old age, or magic, etc., then such a person, with respect to the woman he is unable to have intercourse with, will be considered 'unin (impotent). Al-Fatawa al-Alamgiriyyah, vol. 2. The clarification of the legal definition of 'unin is established from the above statement: according to the terminology of the fuqaha, 'unin (impotent) is a person who, despite having the male organ, is unable to have intercourse (coitus) with a woman, whether this condition arises from illness, weakness, old age, or because someone cast a spell on him. And if someone is able to have intercourse with some women but not with others, then with respect to the woman he is unable to have intercourse with, he is considered 'unin (impotent). (2) The wife of an 'unin (if she wants to obtain a divorce) has the right to separation (tafriq) according to shari'ah under the following conditions. (3) Alif - He said in al-Alamgiriyyah, chapter on al-'Unin: When a woman raises her husband to the judge and claims that he is 'unin and demands separation, the judge asks him whether he has had intercourse with her or not. If he admits that he has not had intercourse, he grants him a respite of one year, whether the woman is a virgin or non-virgin; then his statement (that he has had intercourse) is accepted with his oath, as in al-Bada'i'. If he (the husband) swears, her right is invalidated; if he refuses to swear, he is granted a year's delay, as in al-Kafi. And if she says 'I am a virgin', women examine her; a single woman is sufficient, but two are more cautious and reliable. If they say she is non-virgin, the husband's statement is accepted with his oath, as in al-Siraj al-Wahhaj. (Up to his statement:) 'If they say she is a virgin, her statement is accepted without oath.' And in al-Durr al-Mukhtar: If a trustworthy woman says she is a virgin, then she is given the choice, according to one of two views. (b) Regarding the delay, the lunar year is considered according to the apparent narration, as in al-Tabyin, and that is correct, as in al-Hidayah. And al-Hasan narrated from Abu Hanifah that he is tested for a solar year, which exceeds the lunar by days. This is the madhhab of Shams al-A'imma al-Sarakhsi in Sharh al-Kafi, adopting the narration of al-Hasan based on precaution, and likewise Sahib al-Tuhfah. And this is the chosen view according to me, as in Ghayat al-Bayan. It is the choice of Shams al-A'imma in al-Mabsut, and the choice of Taj al-Islam and Imam Zahir al-Din, and upon it is the fatwa, as in al-Khulasah. (Alamgiri, may Allah forgive him, 215). And in al-Sadr and al-Wabil: no comment. And if in the middle of the month, it is not said that after intercourse? (c) The beginning of the delay is from the time of the complaint, as in al-Muhit. (Rum? page ?). And in Mabsut al-Sarakhsi (Maghrib? volume?) in a long passage, and the period is not written before the page. (d) If the woman comes to the judge after the expiry of the delay and claims that he did not have intercourse with her, and the husband claims that he did, then if she was originally a non-virgin, the husband's statement is accepted with his oath, up to his statement: and if the woman says 'I am a virgin', women examine her, up to his statement: if they say she is a virgin, or the husband admits that he did not have intercourse with her, then the judge (decrees) separation, as in Sharh al-Jami' al-Saghir of Qadi Khan. (Alamgiri, vol. 2). And for him in Radd al-Mukhtar from al-Mi'raj: if he inserted only the glans, he is not 'unin; if he is circumcised, insertion of the remaining part of the penis is necessary. He said in al-Bahr: it is sufficient to suffice with the amount of the missing part (i.e., the glans). Page? volume 132. From it, the intended meaning is the time of the delay, because it is not postponed from the complaint without cause. Thus harmony is achieved between these two narrations, 12 from it.
Source pages p. 78

Commentary
This passage is a legal ruling (rasm rule) defining the term 'unin (impotent) in Hanafi law and detailing the procedure for a wife to seek separation on grounds of impotence. The author, Ashraf Ali Thanwi, quotes from authoritative Hanafi sources. Key points: (1) 'Unin is defined not by absolute inability to perform with all women, but relative to a specific woman. If a man can perform with some women but not with a particular woman due to illness, weakness, old age, or magic, he is considered 'unin concerning that woman. (2) The wife has the right to request separation (tafriq) through the court. (3) The judge first asks the husband; if he admits inability, a one-year respite is granted. The husband's oath that he has had intercourse is accepted, but if he refuses to swear, the delay is imposed. (4) If the wife claims virginity, women inspect her; the result determines whether her statement or the husband's is accepted. (5) There is a difference of opinion on whether the year is lunar or solar. The apparent narration (Zahir al-Riwayah) is lunar, but many later scholars (including al-Sarakhsi) choose solar as a precaution. The fatwa is on the solar year. (6) The delay starts from the time the wife files the complaint. (7) After the year, if the wife still claims non-consummation, the judge decides based on evidence and may decree separation. (8) Partial insertion (only the glans) does not constitute consummation; if the man is circumcised, the entire glans must be inserted. The passage demonstrates Hanafi methodology in defining legal terms and resolving conflicting narrations (e.g., by harmonization or choosing the more precautionary view).

Key Terms
عنین ('unin): A man who, despite having a male organ, is unable to have vaginal intercourse with his wife due to illness, weakness, old age, or magic, whether absolute or relative to that specific woman.
تفریق (tafriq): Judicial separation or annulment of marriage granted by a qadi due to a defect such as impotence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the mufta bihi (relied-upon) position regarding the length of the waiting period for an 'unin husband?
A) Lunar year of 12 lunar months.
B) Solar year of 365 days.
C) 6 months, as per al-Hasan ibn Ziyad.
D) No waiting period; immediate separation.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi definition of 'unin is relative to a specific wife, and the wife's right to tafriq involves a one-year waiting period, with the fatwa position favouring a solar year for precaution.

The One-Year Postponement: Lunar vs Solar Year
Intra-Hanafi dispute on the length of the postponement period

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the relied-upon Hanafi position on the calculation of the one-year waiting period for an impotent husband and explain the tarjih process between the lunar and solar year narrations.

Core Idea
The lunar year is the mufta bihi for the impotence waiting period, not the solar year.

This passage presents a key intra-Hanafi dispute over whether the one-year postponement for an impotent husband should be a lunar or solar year. Understanding how the school resolved this dispute is essential for a mufti who must issue consistent rulings. We will trace the narrations and the tarjih process that settled on the lunar year as the operative position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed issue
The dispute is about the length of the postponement period: is it a lunar year (approximately 354 days) or a solar year (365 days)?
The difference impacts the wife's right to annulment.
2
State the two narrations
The passage mentions: (1) zahir al-riwaya states it is a lunar year (as in al-Hidayah, al-Tabyin); (2) Hasan ibn Ziyad narrates from Abu Hanifa that it is a solar year.
The solar year is considered more cautious (ihtiyat).
3
Follow the tarjih process
Some later scholars (e.g., al-Sarakhsi, author of al-Tuhfah) preferred the solar year for caution, but the majority of muhaqqiqun and the later school authorities adopted the lunar year.
The fatwa is upon the lunar year according to al-Khulasa and others.
4
Identify the mufta bihi
The relied-upon position for fatwa is the lunar year. This is confirmed by multiple reliable sources (al-Hidayah, al-Tabyin, al-Khulasa).
A mufti should not give fatwa based on the solar year except in exceptional circumstances.
5
Note the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: a solar year is longer, which may delay the wife's right to annulment. The school's consistent practice is the lunar year, which provides a shorter waiting period.

Source pages p. 79

Commentary
This passage is a collection of rulings and ikhtilafat concerning the case of an impotent husband (عنن). The primary ruling is that if a wife claims her husband is impotent, the qadi will give the husband one year to treat the condition. There are two narrations regarding the calculation of this year: one from Imam Abu Hanifa (via Hasan ibn Ziyad) that it is a solar year, but the relied-upon position (zahir al-riwaya) is that it is a lunar year, as preferred by many fuqaha. The passage also discusses when the period begins: from the time of the dispute (mukhasama), not from the time of the complaint or the court judgment. Regarding the wife's claim of virginity, if she says she is a virgin, her statement is taken without an oath, but some scholars require examination by women. If she chooses separation, the qadi orders the husband to give a ba'in divorce. The passage also notes that partial insertion does not constitute impotence, and for an amputated person, the remaining part must be inserted. The author synthesizes multiple sources, showing the preferred positions and reconciling apparent contradictions. The fatwa is based on the lunar year calculation and the procedure described.

Key Terms
عنن (unan): Impotence in a husband, inability to perform sexual intercourse.
تأجيل (ta'jil): The one-year postponement granted to the impotent husband to seek treatment.
تفريق (tafriq): Separation ordered by the qadi when the husband fails to cure his impotence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The one-year waiting period for an impotent husband is a lunar year according to the mufta bihi, not a solar year.

Procedure for the Wife Seeking Annulment Due to Impotence
Procedural rulings for impotence annulment

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to describe the procedural steps a wife must follow when seeking annulment due to her husband's impotence, including when the delay begins, how her claim of virginity is treated, and the final ruling.

Core Idea
The one-year delay begins from the wife's complaint, and her claim of virginity is accepted without an oath.

This passage details the practical steps of the impotence annulment procedure. It covers the husband's oath, the wife's claim of virginity, the start of the postponement, the definition of impotence in terms of partial insertion, and the legal outcome—a ba'in divorce. These rules help the mufti guide the qadi in such cases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the initial step when the wife complains
After the postponement period expires, the wife comes to the qadi and alleges that the husband did not have intercourse with her. The husband may claim he did.
The dispute must be brought to the qadi; the period does not start automatically.
2
Determine whose statement is taken
If the wife was originally a virgin at the time of marriage, the husband's statement with his oath is preferred. If the wife claims she is still a virgin, female witnesses may examine her.
This rule creates a presumption based on the wife's original status.
3
Know when the delay begins
The one-year postponement starts from the time of the dispute (mukhasama), i.e., when the wife files her complaint, not from the wedding or the qadi's order.
This is stated in al-Muhit.
4
Understand the definition of impotence
Partial insertion (only the glans) does not constitute impotence. If the husband's penis is amputated, he must insert the remaining part to be considered capable.
The passage cites al-Bahr: one should suffice with the amount of an amputated organ.
5
Know the final outcome
If after the delay the husband still cannot perform, the qadi orders a ba'in divorce (irrevocable separation). This is because no actual consummation occurred.
The separation is considered one ba'in divorce.

Source pages p. 79

Commentary
This passage is a collection of rulings and ikhtilafat concerning the case of an impotent husband (عنن). The primary ruling is that if a wife claims her husband is impotent, the qadi will give the husband one year to treat the condition. There are two narrations regarding the calculation of this year: one from Imam Abu Hanifa (via Hasan ibn Ziyad) that it is a solar year, but the relied-upon position (zahir al-riwaya) is that it is a lunar year, as preferred by many fuqaha. The passage also discusses when the period begins: from the time of the dispute (mukhasama), not from the time of the complaint or the court judgment. Regarding the wife's claim of virginity, if she says she is a virgin, her statement is taken without an oath, but some scholars require examination by women. If she chooses separation, the qadi orders the husband to give a ba'in divorce. The passage also notes that partial insertion does not constitute impotence, and for an amputated person, the remaining part must be inserted. The author synthesizes multiple sources, showing the preferred positions and reconciling apparent contradictions. The fatwa is based on the lunar year calculation and the procedure described.

Key Terms
عنن (unan): Impotence in a husband, inability to perform sexual intercourse.
تأجيل (ta'jil): The one-year postponement granted to the impotent husband to seek treatment.
تفريق (tafriq): Separation ordered by the qadi when the husband fails to cure his impotence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) From the date the husband admits his condition

Key Takeaway
The one-year postponement for an impotent husband begins from the complaint, and failure leads to a ba'in divorce.

Impotence and the Wife's Right to Separation under Hanafi Law
Explaining the wife's right to annulment and husband's oath procedure

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi procedure for a wife seeking separation due to impotence and identify when the husband's oath prevents the separation.

Core Idea
The wife can seek annulment for impotence, but the husband's oath can block the separation.

This lesson examines the Shari'ah mechanism provided for a wife married to an impotent husband. The Hanafi school elaborates a specific multi-step procedure that protects both parties — the woman from an unfulfilled marriage and the man from hasty judgment. The source passage from major Hanafi references outlines the ruling, the judge's role, and the crucial effect of the husband's oath.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal issue
This is a legal stratagem (hila) for the wife to exit a marriage where the husband is impotent. Understand that the option is given to the wife, not automatic termination.
Impotence (عنن) gives the wife the right to seek judicial separation.
2
Trace the procedure
Wife goes to qadi → husband gets one-year respite for treatment → if still impotent, qadi orders divorce. This sequence ensures fairness.
The one-year period is for the husband to seek medical treatment.
3
Understand the oath scenario
If the husband claims he has consummated and the wife does not claim virginity, the husband's oath is taken. If he swears, the wife loses her right to separation.
The oath is a decisive factor; the wife is not required to swear.
4
Determine the type of separation
The divorce ordered by the qadi is bāʾin (irrevocable) because it is treated as a separation before actual consummation.
Bāʾin divorce ends the marriage immediately; the husband cannot take her back without a new marriage.

From the above statement, the procedure for the wife of an impotent man and the decision is known: that the wife of the impotent person should present her case to the qadi's court and say: 'Indeed I was not able to have intercourse with this woman.' Then that person will be given a one-year respite for treatment. If he does not confess but claims intercourse, then the detail is: if the woman does not claim virginity, then the man will be required to take an oath. If he takes the oath, then the wife will not have the right to separation.
Source pages p. 80

Commentary
This passage presents the Hanafi ruling on impotence (عنن) as a legal stratagem for the wife to obtain separation. The wife first brings her case to the qadi, claiming her husband is unable to consummate. The husband is given one year to seek treatment. If he claims he did consummate but the wife does not claim virginity, his oath is accepted, and the wife loses her right to separation. The Arabic passages affirm that if the wife chooses separation, the judge orders an irrevocable divorce (طلاق بائن). The reasoning is that this separation occurs before actual consummation, hence it is irrevocable. The Urdu commentary details the procedure step by step.

Key Terms
خيرت (khuyyirat): The wife is given the choice (to either remain or seek separation) in case of husband's impotence.
لا تستحلف (lā tustaḥlaf): The wife is not required to take an oath (to support her claim).
فرقة (furqa): Separation (annulment or divorce) between spouses.
بائنة (bāʾina): Irrevocable divorce; the husband cannot take the wife back without a new marriage.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, a wife can seek separation for impotence, but the procedure includes a one-year respite for the husband and his oath may bar the separation.

Medical Examination in Impotence Cases: Hanafi and Maliki Rules
Procedure for medical examination when wife claims virginity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule on medical examination in impotence cases and evaluate the inter-madhhab dispute with the Maliki school when issuing a fatwa.

Core Idea
One trustworthy woman suffices for Hanafi examination; two are needed in Maliki — know your forum.

In cases where a wife claims virginity to prove her husband's impotence, a medical examination may be necessary. This lesson details the Hanafi procedure for such an examination and highlights a critical inter-madhhab difference: the number of women required to conduct it. Understanding this dispute is essential for a mufti, especially when a case is decided by a non-Hanafi body such as a shari'a panchayat.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario triggering the examination
The medical examination is needed when the wife claims to be a virgin (bākira) and the husband has either refused to take an oath or his impotence is contested.
If the wife does not claim virginity, the husband's oath alone suffices.
2
State the Hanafi rule on number of examiners
According to the Hanafi school, one upright, experienced woman is sufficient for the examination. Caution suggests two women, but one is legally adequate based on istihsan.
The Hanafi distinction between shahāda (formal testimony) and ikhbār (mere information) allows one woman's report as evidence in such matters.
3
Recognize the Maliki objection
Imam Malik (as cited in the passage) requires at least two women for all testimonies, including medical examinations. One woman's testimony is not permissible in any matter.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf rooted in different usul al-fiqh principles.
4
Apply the conflict when the deciding body is non-Hanafi
If the case is decided by a shari'a panchayat or any body that follows the Maliki school, the Maliki rule must be adopted: two women are required. A mufti must advise accordingly.
This is a practical example of thamarah al-khilaf (the fruit of disagreement) affecting fatwa issuance.
5
Distinguish outcomes based on examination results
If the examining women report that the wife is not a virgin, the husband must take an oath that he consummated; if he swears, his word is accepted and she loses the right to separation. If he refuses, one year's respite for treatment is given. If the women report she is still a virgin, the qadi gives the husband one year's respite without any oath.
The results determine whether the husband's oath or the wife's claim prevails.

Issue of medical examination of the woman. If the husband refuses to take an oath, then he is given a one-year period for treatment... 'I said: regarding matters not seen by men, is the testimony of one woman permissible? The second scenario is that the women report concerning the wife's virginity that she is still a virgin, then the qadi, without requiring any oath, gives the impotent man one year's respite. Summary: When it is proven by any evidence that the woman is not a virgin but non-virgin—whether her non-virginity is known through this method, that she was a widow and had children from the first husband, or by her own confession, or by examination of women—in these three situations, the man's statement with oath is accepted that he has consummated, and the woman is not given the right to separation. And if in the three situations the man refuses to take an oath, then the woman's claim is deemed correct and one year's respite is given. And if from examination of women the wife's [virginity]...
Source pages p. 80

Commentary
This passage continues the hila (legal stratagem) for the wife of an impotent man, focusing on the medical examination when the wife claims virginity. The author first notes that if the husband refuses to take an oath, he is given a one-year respite for treatment. If the case is decided by a non-qadi body (panchayat), it must follow the Maliki school, which requires two women for examination. The Hanafi position is that one trustworthy woman suffices, but caution suggests two. After examination, two scenarios arise: if the women report she is not a virgin, the husband is required to swear he consummated; if he swears, his word prevails and the wife loses her right to separation. If he refuses, he gets one year. If the women report she is still a virgin, the qadi gives the husband one year without any oath. The summary reinforces that if any evidence (prior marriage, confession, examination) shows she is not a virgin, the husband's oath is accepted; otherwise, the wife's claim stands.

Key Terms
معائنہ (muʿāyana): Physical examination (of the wife to determine virginity).
باکرہ (bākira): Virgin (a woman who has not had sexual intercourse).
شهادة (shahāda): Testimony; in this context, the report of the examining women.
اخبار (ikhbār): Informing, giving information (as distinct from formal testimony).

Legal Stratagem for Impotence: Procedure and the Lunar/Solar Year Dispute
Explains the procedure when wife claims husband's impotence, including examination, oath, and one-year waiting period.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct the Hanafi legal stratagem for a wife claiming impotence, including the examination procedure, oath deferment, and the relied-upon fatwa on the year calculation.

Core Idea
The impotence stratagem gives a wife a path to annulment but requires a one-year deferment and careful evidentiary procedure.

Marriage is about companionship and intimacy. When a husband is unable to consummate, the wife may seek annulment. Hanafi fiqh provides a detailed hila to resolve this. This lesson unpacks the steps: female examination, husband's oath, one-year deferment, and the crucial fatwa preference for the solar year.

Guided Path
1
Examine the wife's claim and status
Identify whether the wife claims to be a virgin or not. This determines the next step—either require an oath from the husband or proceed directly to deferment.
The examination is done by female experts, preferably two just women.
2
Apply the oath or direct deferment
If the wife is not a virgin (e.g., widow or previous intercourse), the husband is asked to swear he consummated. If he swears, her claim is rejected; if he refuses, a one-year deferment is given. If she is a virgin, no oath is needed—immediate deferment.
The husband's oath is only valid when the wife's non-virginity is established by evidence.
3
Understand the year calculation dispute
The text presents an ikhtilaf: Zahir al-Riwayah uses a lunar year, but the riwaya of Hasan ibn Ziyad and later Hanafi muftis prefer the solar year for caution and custom.
This dispute is resolved by the muta'akhkhirun who gave fatwa on the solar year.
4
Identify the mufta bihi (operative ruling)
The relied-upon position for fatwa is the solar year, starting from the judge's order. If the husband recovers within that year, the wife loses her right to annulment permanently.
Check al-Fatawa al-Hindiyya for the standard fatwa.
5
Apply the outcome after the year
If after one year the husband admits inability, the judge gives the wife the option to annul. If he recovers and consummates even once, her right is forfeited.
The right is permanently lost, not deferred again.

ملاح
ایک سال میں بھی ارمنین ہمبستری نہ کر سکے؟ اگر شوہر طابع دی گئی برت (ایک سال) میں بہتری نہ کر سکا تو عورت کے دوبارہ درخواست کرنے پر قاضی تحقیق کرے اور اگر شوہر نے اقرار کر لیا کہ میں ہمبستری پر قادر نہیں ہوا ، جب تو عورت کا دھوئی واضح طور پر سمجھ ہو گیا اس صورت میں قاضی عورت کو اختیار دے دے
And if the woman claims to be a virgin, the judge shall have her examined by women. The examination of one just, experienced woman is sufficient, but caution requires that two just women examine. Then after examination, there are two cases: The first case: the women state that she is not a virgin. Then the husband is made to swear that he had intercourse with her. If he swears, his statement is accepted and the wife loses the right to separation. If he refuses to swear, a one-year deferment is ordered for his treatment. The second case: the women report that she is still a virgin. Then the judge, without requiring an oath, gives the impotent man a one-year deferment for treatment. In summary: When it is established by any evidence that the woman is not a virgin—whether through being a widow with children from a previous husband, or by her own admission, or by the women's examination—in these three situations, the man's statement with oath is accepted that he has had intercourse, and the woman is not given the right to separation. If in these three situations the man refuses the oath, the woman's claim is considered valid and she is given a one-year deferment. If by the women's examination the wife's virginity is proven, then without an oath a one-year deferment is given. This detail is mentioned in al-Fatawa al-Hindiyya, letter 'Alif'. Regarding the calculation of the year for the treatment period: In Zahir al-Riwayah, the lunar year is considered. But in the riwaya of Hasan, the solar year (i.e., Christian year) is considered, and some preferers have adopted it out of caution. The later scholars (muta'akhkhirun) generally gave fatwa upon it, as mentioned in al-Fatawa al-Hindiyya, letter 'Ba'. Now also the general practice of the people of fatwa is upon it. This year is considered to start from the time the judge gives the deferment; any previous period, however long, is not counted, as understood from the texts of al-Hindiyya and al-Mabsut, letter 'Jim'. Then, if within this year the husband recovers through any means and becomes capable of intercourse, and performs intercourse even once, the wife no longer has the right to annul the marriage; rather, this right is permanently nullified, and she can never demand separation. If the husband cannot perform intercourse within the one year, then upon the wife's re-application, the judge investigates. If the husband admits that he is not capable of intercourse, then the wife's claim is clearly established. In this case, the judge gives her the option to annul.
Source pages p. 81

Commentary
This passage is a hila (legal stratagem) case dealing with the issue of impotence ('innah) in marriage. It outlines the Hanafi procedure for a wife who claims her husband is impotent and seeks annulment of the marriage (faskh). The passage first describes the examination of the wife by female experts to determine virginity. If she is not a virgin, the husband is asked to swear that he has consummated the marriage; if he swears, the wife's claim is rejected. If he refuses, a one-year waiting period is granted. If she is still a virgin, the husband is given a one-year deferment without an oath. The summary clarifies three scenarios where the husband's oath is accepted: the wife is a widow, she admits to previous intercourse, or examination shows non-virginity. The heart of the hila is the one-year deferment to allow treatment, after which if the husband recovers, the wife's right to annulment is lost. The passage then discusses a critical point of ikhtilaf: whether the year is lunar or solar. The Zahir al-Riwayah (the established transmission) uses the lunar year, but the riwaya of Hasan ibn Ziyad (a nawadir transmission) prefers the solar year, and the later scholars (muta'akhkhirun) have adopted the solar year for fatwa, as it is more cautious and practical. The year begins from the judge's order. This is a clear example of talfiq (combining different transmissions) based on need (hajah). The passage concludes with the procedure after the year: if the husband recovers, the right is permanently lost; if not, and he admits failure, the judge gives the wife the option to annul. This hila resolves the gridlock of a wife stuck in a non-consummated marriage without proof.

Key Terms
عنن ('innah): Impotence; the husband's inability to perform intercourse, which gives the wife a right to seek annulment after a one-year waiting period.
ہمبستری (hambistari): Sexual intercourse; the act that consummates the marriage. The husband's ability or inability to perform it is central to the case of impotence.
مہلت (muhlat): The one-year deferment granted to the impotent husband to seek treatment before the wife can demand annulment.
ظاہر الروایہ (Zahir al-Riwayah): The six core books of Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani, which are the most authoritative transmissions in the Hanafi school.
حسن کی روایت (Riwayat Hasan): The transmission of Hasan ibn Ziyad, which is from the Nawadir (rare transmissions) and has lesser authority but may be followed for fatwa when custom or need requires.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi legal stratagem for impotence requires a one-year deferment (solar year by fatwa) from the judge's order; the wife's right to annulment is permanent if the husband recovers within that year, but if he fails, she may annul.

The One-Year Respite for the 'Inan: Calculation and Starting Point
Explaining the duration, start, and calculation of the respite.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the correct starting point of the one-year respite for an impotent husband and explain the preferred calculation (solar vs lunar) according to the later Hanafi fatwa practice.

Core Idea
The one-year respite starts from the judge's ruling and is counted in solar years per later Hanafi fatwa.

This lesson examines the legal mechanics of the one-year respite (muhlat) granted to a husband proven impotent. The Hanafi school provides detailed guidance on when the year begins and whether it should be calculated using the lunar or solar calendar, with the later authorities preferring the solar year for practical ease.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling scenario
The passage deals with a husband whose impotence is proven. The judge grants a one-year respite to allow treatment.
2
Determine the start time of the respite
The year is counted from the moment the judge grants the respite, not from the marriage date or any earlier period.
Time before the ruling is not counted regardless of how long it has passed.
3
Evaluate the dispute on year calculation: lunar vs solar
Zahir al-Riwayah uses the lunar year, but a riwaya from Hasan ibn Ziyad and later scholars prefer the solar year. The later scholars, including the compilers of Fatawa Alamgiri, gave fatwa on the solar year.
This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf resolved by later practice.
4
Apply the tarjih (preferred position)
The mufta bihi position is the solar year, as adopted by the majority of later scholars and current fatwa practice.
The practical difference: the solar year is slightly longer, giving more time for treatment.

If it is proven that he is impotent, then a respite of one year will be given without [any further] respite. This detail is mentioned in al-Fatawa al-Alamgiriyya, letter alif. Regarding the calculation of the year, the apparent riwaya is to consider the lunar year, but in the riwaya of Hasan, the solar year, i.e., the Christian year, is considered, and some preferring scholars have cautiously adopted that. And the later scholars generally gave fatwa on that, as mentioned in al-Fatawa al-Alamgiriyya, letter ba. Even now, the general practice of the people of fatwa is this. And this year will be considered to start from the time the judge grants the respite. Whatever period passed before that will not be considered, as understood from the expressions of al-Alamgiriyya and al-Mabsut, letter jim. Then, during this year, if the husband becomes healthy through some treatment and becomes capable of intercourse and has intercourse with his wife even once, then the wife's right to annul the marriage is no longer. Rather, this right is abrogated forever, and the wife can never demand separation again. If the husband cannot have intercourse even within one year? If the husband could not improve during the appointed period (one year), then upon the wife's renewed request, the judge will investigate, and if the husband admits that he became unable to have intercourse, then the wife's claim becomes clear. In this case, the judge will give the wife the option: if you need separation, i.e., divorce, then obtain separation, otherwise prefer to stay with your husband. If that woman wishes to obtain separation (divorce) in that very sitting, then the husband will be made to divorce her; if the husband refuses, then the judge will separate them. As the detail will come later. If the husband claims that he had intercourse? If the husband denies having intercourse but claims that intercourse did occur, then the detail is as follows: if at the time of granting the respite it was proven that the woman was not a virgin, or now the woman admits that her virginity has been removed in some way (i.e., she is not a virgin) but intercourse did not occur, then in this case the husband will be made to take an oath. If the husband swears an oath saying, 'I have had intercourse with this woman,' then the husband's word will be accepted, and separation cannot occur between them? As mentioned later. Regarding the oath of the impotent husband: if the husband refuses to take an oath at that time as well, then the woman will be given the right to demand separation (i.e., the claim of divorce). And if at the time of granting the respite (through examination) it was proven that the woman was a virgin, and now upon re-examination (medical checkup) the woman's virginity is confirmed, then also without taking an oath from the woman, the judge will give her the option to remain in the marriage with her husband or to demand separation. And in those cases where the judge gives the wife the option...
Source pages p. 82

Commentary
This passage is a legal ruling from the Hanafi school regarding the case of an impotent husband (عنّین). It details the procedure for the wife to seek annulment of the marriage (فسخ نکاح). The key points: First, if impotence is proven, the judge grants a one-year respite. The preferred view (and that of later scholars) is to count the year as a solar year, starting from the date the judge grants the respite. Any time before that is not counted. During that year, if the husband becomes potent and has intercourse even once, the wife's right to annulment is permanently lost. If after a year he is still impotent, the judge gives the wife the option to either stay or separate. If the husband claims he did have intercourse, the procedure depends on whether the wife's virginity status: if she was a non-virgin at the time of respite, or admits loss of virginity, then the husband's oath is taken; if he swears, his word is accepted. If she was a virgin and remains so, the judge grants the option without requiring an oath from the wife. This ruling is based on the principle that the wife has a right to a normal marital relationship, and if the husband cannot fulfill that, she may seek annulment after a defined period. This follows the Hanafi methodology of providing a legal stratagem (hila) to resolve marital hardship.

Key Terms
عنین ('inan): An impotent man who cannot have intercourse
مہلت (muhlat): The one-year respite granted by the judge to the impotent husband to seek treatment
ظاہر الروایہ (zahir al-riwayah): The well-known transmitted rulings from the early Hanafi masters
مشکل (mufta bihi): The relied-upon position in fatwa

A) Lunar calendar
B) Solar calendar
C) Both are equally valid
D) It depends on the judge's discretion

Key Takeaway
The one-year respite for the impotent husband begins from the judge's ruling and is counted using the solar calendar according to the preferred Hanafi fatwa.

The Effects After the Respite: Potency, Impotence, and Claims
Detailing the consequences of the respite and the procedural steps for the wife and judge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rules for when the husband becomes potent during the year or remains impotent after it, and distinguish the procedures when the husband claims intercourse.

Core Idea
If the husband becomes potent and has intercourse once, the wife's right to annulment is lost forever; if not, she gets the option after one year.

This lesson continues the ruling on the impotent husband by exploring what happens during and after the one-year respite. The key turning point is whether the husband can achieve intercourse even once. The passage also details the evidentiary rules when the husband claims he did have intercourse, involving oaths and examining the wife's virginity.

Guided Path
1
Understand the effect of successful treatment during the year
If the husband becomes potent and has intercourse even once within the year, the wife's right to faskh is permanently abrogated.
This applies regardless of whether the wife consented or not.
2
Study the procedure after the year if impotence persists
If the husband remains impotent, upon the wife's renewed request, the judge gives her the option to either stay or seek separation. If she chooses separation, the judge first tries to have the husband divorce her; if he refuses, the judge separates them.
The wife's option is exercisable in the same sitting.
3
Examine the case where the husband claims intercourse
If the husband denies impotence and claims he did have intercourse, the procedure depends on the wife's virginity status at the time of respite. If she was a non-virgin or admits loss of virginity (by any means), the husband is made to take an oath; if he swears, his claim is accepted and no separation occurs.
If the wife is a confirmed virgin at the time of respite and remains so, the judge gives her the option without requiring an oath from her.
4
Analyze the oath refusal scenario
If the husband refuses to take the oath when required, the wife is given the right to demand separation.
Refusal tantamount to admission.

If it is proven that he is impotent, then a respite of one year will be given without [any further] respite. This detail is mentioned in al-Fatawa al-Alamgiriyya, letter alif. Regarding the calculation of the year, the apparent riwaya is to consider the lunar year, but in the riwaya of Hasan, the solar year, i.e., the Christian year, is considered, and some preferring scholars have cautiously adopted that. And the later scholars generally gave fatwa on that, as mentioned in al-Fatawa al-Alamgiriyya, letter ba. Even now, the general practice of the people of fatwa is this. And this year will be considered to start from the time the judge grants the respite. Whatever period passed before that will not be considered, as understood from the expressions of al-Alamgiriyya and al-Mabsut, letter jim. Then, during this year, if the husband becomes healthy through some treatment and becomes capable of intercourse and has intercourse with his wife even once, then the wife's right to annul the marriage is no longer. Rather, this right is abrogated forever, and the wife can never demand separation again. If the husband cannot have intercourse even within one year? If the husband could not improve during the appointed period (one year), then upon the wife's renewed request, the judge will investigate, and if the husband admits that he became unable to have intercourse, then the wife's claim becomes clear. In this case, the judge will give the wife the option: if you need separation, i.e., divorce, then obtain separation, otherwise prefer to stay with your husband. If that woman wishes to obtain separation (divorce) in that very sitting, then the husband will be made to divorce her; if the husband refuses, then the judge will separate them. As the detail will come later. If the husband claims that he had intercourse? If the husband denies having intercourse but claims that intercourse did occur, then the detail is as follows: if at the time of granting the respite it was proven that the woman was not a virgin, or now the woman admits that her virginity has been removed in some way (i.e., she is not a virgin) but intercourse did not occur, then in this case the husband will be made to take an oath. If the husband swears an oath saying, 'I have had intercourse with this woman,' then the husband's word will be accepted, and separation cannot occur between them? As mentioned later. Regarding the oath of the impotent husband: if the husband refuses to take an oath at that time as well, then the woman will be given the right to demand separation (i.e., the claim of divorce). And if at the time of granting the respite (through examination) it was proven that the woman was a virgin, and now upon re-examination (medical checkup) the woman's virginity is confirmed, then also without taking an oath from the woman, the judge will give her the option to remain in the marriage with her husband or to demand separation. And in those cases where the judge gives the wife the option...
Source pages p. 82

Commentary
This passage is a legal ruling from the Hanafi school regarding the case of an impotent husband (عنّین). It details the procedure for the wife to seek annulment of the marriage (فسخ نکاح). The key points: First, if impotence is proven, the judge grants a one-year respite. The preferred view (and that of later scholars) is to count the year as a solar year, starting from the date the judge grants the respite. Any time before that is not counted. During that year, if the husband becomes potent and has intercourse even once, the wife's right to annulment is permanently lost. If after a year he is still impotent, the judge gives the wife the option to either stay or separate. If the husband claims he did have intercourse, the procedure depends on whether the wife's virginity status: if she was a non-virgin at the time of respite, or admits loss of virginity, then the husband's oath is taken; if he swears, his word is accepted. If she was a virgin and remains so, the judge grants the option without requiring an oath from the wife. This ruling is based on the principle that the wife has a right to a normal marital relationship, and if the husband cannot fulfill that, she may seek annulment after a defined period. This follows the Hanafi methodology of providing a legal stratagem (hila) to resolve marital hardship.

Key Terms
اختیار (ikhtiyar): The option given by the judge to the wife to stay or separate after the year
حلف (half): Oath taken by the husband when he claims intercourse under specific conditions
ثیبہ (thayyib): A woman who is not a virgin (previously married or sexually experienced)
بکر (bikr): A virgin

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What happens to the wife's right to annulment if the husband has intercourse once during the one-year respite?
A) She can still seek annulment after the year ends.
B) Her right to annulment is permanently lost.
C) She must wait another year to see if he remains potent.
D) She can seek annulment only if the husband agrees.

Key Takeaway
The husband's achieving intercourse during the year permanently extinguishes the wife's right to annulment; after the year, if impotence persists, the wife may choose separation through judicial process.

Wife's Separation Due to Husband's Impotence: Conditions and Procedure
Detailed rules for a wife to obtain separation when husband is impotent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi conditions for a wife to obtain judicial separation (tafriq) due to her husband's impotence ('innah), and identify the mufta bihi position regarding prior knowledge of the defect.

Core Idea
Impotence gives a wife the right to separation only if she did not know at marriage and he never consummated; the choice must be exercised in the same session.

This passage from al-Hilat al-Najizah outlines the legal stratagem (hila) for a wife to exit a marriage with an impotent husband ('innen). The Hanafi school provides a detailed procedure involving the husband's oath, medical examination, and judicial separation. Understanding these conditions is crucial for issuing sound fatwas in family law cases. Let's walk through the rules step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the wife's primary choice
The text begins by stating that the wife may either seek separation or remain with her husband. If she chooses separation, she must declare it in the same session (majlis) before the judge.
2
Analyze the dispute when husband claims intercourse
If the husband claims that he did consummate, the wife's virginity status becomes key. If she was proven virgin at the time of respite or is confirmed virgin by medical exam, the husband is made to take an oath. If he swears, his word is accepted.
3
Determine the procedure when husband refuses oath
If the husband refuses to take the oath, the wife is given the option to demand separation. However, she must exercise this option in the same session; otherwise, she loses the right.
4
Apply the first condition: wife's knowledge at marriage
A critical condition: if the wife knew of the husband's impotence at the time of nikah, she has no right to litigate. This is the mufta bihi position, as per Fatawa Alamgiri and al-Durr al-Mukhtar.
There is a second opinion, but the fatwa is on the first.
5
Apply the second condition: non-consummation
The husband must never have had intercourse with the wife even once. If he has, the right to separation is lost regardless of impotence.

Source Text

If you need separation (talaq) due to impotence, then procure separation, otherwise prefer to stay with your husband. If that woman wishes to obtain separation (talaq) in that same session, then let talaq be procured from the husband; if the husband refuses, then the judge shall separate between them. As will be detailed ahead. If the husband claims actual intercourse? If the husband refuses to admit impotence but rather claims intercourse occurred, then the detail is: if at the time of granting respite the woman's non-virginity was proven, or now the woman admits that somehow her hymen was removed (i.e., she is a virgin) but intercourse did not occur, then in this case the husband shall be made to take an oath; if the husband swears saying, 'I have had intercourse with this woman,' then his statement shall be accepted and separation between them cannot occur, as will be mentioned ahead. Regarding the oath of the impotent man: if the husband also refuses to take the oath at that time, then the woman shall be given the right to demand separation (i.e., the claim of talaq). And if at the time of granting respite (by examination) the woman's virginity (i.e., being a virgin) was proven, and now upon re-examination (medical check-up) the woman's virginity is again confirmed, then even without the husband's oath, the judge shall give the woman the option to remain in marriage with her husband or demand separation. And in those cases where the judge gives the woman the option, the ruling is that if the woman wishes to obtain separation in that same session, then separation can occur, otherwise not. As is mentioned in the conditions (in the following text). So if the woman in that same session declares separation, i.e., she says, 'I have taken the option, I want to be separate from this husband,' then the judge shall say to her husband, 'Give this woman talaq.' If the husband gives talaq, then a ba'in talaq occurs. If he refuses to give talaq, then the judge himself shall effect separation, i.e., in this manner: he says, 'I have separated you from his marriage.' This separation also stands in place of ba'in talaq in Shariah, as is mentioned in Shami and Fatawa Alamgiri. Conditions of separation of an impotent man: detail of conditions for the wife of an impotent man to be separated. The first condition is that before marriage the woman should not have known of his impotence (being a eunuch). If she knew at that time and despite knowledge married him, then she cannot get the right of separation. As in Fatawa Alamgiri, vol. 2, p. 15: 'If the woman knew at the time of marriage that he was impotent and cannot have intercourse with women, she has no right to litigate.' And in al-Durr al-Mukhtar: 'He married a woman or another woman aware of the condition... upon the relied-upon fatwa of the school... contrary to the second opinion.' If the impotent man once has intercourse? The second condition is that after marriage he never had intercourse with that woman.
Source pages p. 83

Commentary
This passage from al-Hilat al-Najizah by Ashraf Ali Thanwi deals with the legal stratagem (hila) for a wife to obtain separation (tafriq) when her husband is impotent ('innen). The text opens by stating the wife's choice: she can either seek separation or remain with her husband. The procedure is that if she chooses separation in the same session, the husband is asked to give talaq; if he refuses, the judge (qadi) can annul the marriage. The passage then addresses a potential dispute: if the husband claims he actually had intercourse (thereby denying impotence), the matter hinges on whether the wife was proven virgin (bikr) at the time of the waiting period (mahlah). If she was virgin and still is, the husband is made to swear an oath. If he swears, his word is accepted and no separation occurs. If he refuses to swear, the wife gets the option to demand separation. However, if the wife's virginity is confirmed both initially and later by medical examination, the judge gives her the option without an oath from the husband. The key ruling is that the wife's choice must be exercised in the same session (majlis); otherwise, she loses the right. If she declares separation, the husband is first urged to give talaq; if he does, it becomes a ba'in talaq (irrevocable divorce). If he refuses, the judge pronounces separation, which has the legal effect of a ba'in talaq. The passage then lists conditions: first, the wife must not have known of the husband's impotence at the time of marriage. If she knew, she has no right to seek separation (as per Fatawa Alamgiri). The text also references a difference of opinion in al-Durr al-Mukhtar about whether the fatwa is on the first opinion or the second. The second condition is that the husband never had intercourse with her even once. This is a classic Hanafi ruling on 'innah, detailed in standard Hanafi fiqh works like Radd al-Muhtar and Fatawa Alamgiri.

Key Terms
عنین ('innen): An impotent man who cannot have sexual intercourse with women.
تفریق (tafriq): Judicial separation between spouses due to a defect (like impotence); it legally stands as a ba'in talaq.
بائنہ (ba'inah): An irrevocable divorce that terminates the marriage immediately and cannot be revoked; remarriage requires a new nikah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi fiqh as stated in this passage, if a woman knew at the time of marriage that her husband was impotent, what is her right to seek separation?
A) She can seek separation immediately.
B) She has no right to seek separation.
C) She can seek separation only if the husband refuses to take an oath.
D) She can seek separation only after one year.

Key Takeaway
The wife's right to separation due to impotence is conditional on her ignorance at marriage and non-consummation; the choice must be made in the same session, and the mufta bihi bars the right if she knew.

Conditions for Annulment Due to Impotence
Compilation of Hanafi fatwa rules on faskh due to impotence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the four conditions for a wife to seek annulment due to impotence and evaluate the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the need for a qadi's ruling.

Core Idea
Impotence gives the wife a conditional option: must not have had intercourse, must not consent, must act in the same sitting, and may need qadi's validation.

This passage consolidates key Hanafi rulings on annulment (faskh) due to impotence, drawing from major sources like Durr Mukhtar, Bada'i, and Alamgiri. A mufti must know the precise conditions under which a wife can seek separation, the procedure for exercising her option, and which opinion to rely upon when there is disagreement within the school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the cause of annulment
The cause is the husband's impotence (inanah), defined as inability to perform intercourse. The first condition regarding prior intercourse: if the husband has had intercourse even once before becoming impotent, the wife loses her right to annulment.
This is because her right to intercourse was fulfilled by that one instance.
2
List the conditions for the wife to retain her right
The passage enumerates conditions: no prior intercourse, no express consent to stay after learning of condition (silence is not consent), wife must be adult and free from physical barriers (rutaq/qaran), and she must request separation or have a guardian do so if insane.
Explicit verbal consent eliminates the right; silence or continued cohabitation does not.
3
Follow the khiyar procedure
After a one-year waiting period, the qadi gives the wife an option (khiyar) in a sitting. She must exercise it in that same sitting. If she leaves, or the qadi stands, or she expresses consent, the option is lost permanently.
The option is a specific 'khiyar al-majlis' – it must be exercised immediately.
4
Examine the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Imam Abu Hanifa requires a qadi's ruling (qada) even after the wife chooses annulment. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold that her choice alone effects separation. The author notes Abu Hanifa's view is the relied-upon (mufta bihi).
The thamarah al-khilaf: under Abu Hanifa, a formal qadi verdict is needed; under Sahibayn, the wife is already separated.
5
Determine the mufta bihi position
The author states that Imam Abu Hanifa's position is the correct one in the reliable books and the most sound in narrations. Therefore, a mufti should advise that a qadi's ruling is necessary after the wife's choice.
This is a clear example of tarjih within the Hanafi school.
