اور مسئلہ کی مکمل وضاحت اور اس کے تمام قیود و شرائط علماء مالکیہ سے ہی معلوم ہو سکتے تھے اس وجہ سے اس ضرورت کا احساس کر کے مسلک امام مالک کے مفتیانِ کرام کی خدمت میں مدینہ منورہ (زادها الله شرفا و تعظیما) تفصیلی طور پر سوال بھیجا گیا ، وہاں کے متعدد علماء محققین نے نہایت تفصیل اور وضاحت کے ساتھ جوابات تحریر فرمائے تھے لیکن پھر اُن میں کچھ شبہات باقی رہے اور بعض نئے سوالات پیدا ہوئے اس وجہ سے اُن مفتیانِ کرام کو دوبارہ جواب تحریر کرنے کی زحمت دی گئی۔ دوبارہ جوابات کے بعد بھی کچھ مزید سوالات کی ضرورت ہوئی تو تیسری مرتبہ اُن حضرات کی خدمت میں سوالات ارسال کر کے جوابات لیے گئے۔ یہ تمام خط وکتابت مدرسہ امداد العلوم تھانہ بھون میں محفوظ ہے اور ان فتاویٰ کا مجموعہ اس کتاب کے آخر میں شریک اشاعت کر دیا گیا ہے ان فتاوی کی جس جس عبارت سے ہمارے سوالات کا جواب نکلتا ہے اُن تمام پر الفاظ سے نمبر ڈال دیئے گئے ہیں اور جوابات مندرجہ ذیل میں ان عبارات کے حوالہ پر اکتفاء کیا گیا ہے کیونکہ عوام کو تو عربی عبارات کی ضرورت نہیں اور اہل علم حضرات اس نمبر کے حوالہ سے آخر رسالہ میں استدلال کی عبارت خود ملا حظہ سکتے ہیں اب سوالات اور جوابات اُردو میں یہاں پیش کئے جاتے ہیں۔
کیا فرماتے ہیں علماء مالکیہ مالکی مفتیان کرام کی خدمت میں سوال نامہ مندرجہ ذیل مسائل میں
(۱) جو شخص مفقود الخبر (لاپتہ) ہو اور تحقیق و تفتیش کے بعد اُس کے بارے میں معلوم نہ ہو کہ زندہ ہے یا مر گیا، کیا اس شخص کی بیوی کے لیے حق ہے کہ وہ کسی طریقہ سے خود کو اُس شخص کے نکاح سے نکال کر دوسرا نکاح کر سکے؟ اگر اس کو یہ حق ہے تو کیا اس عورت کو کچھ زمانہ انتظار کرنے کی ضرورت ہے یا اس کو بغیر مہلت کے اختیار دے دیا جائے گا؟
(۳) اگر ایسی عورت کو مہلت دی جائے گی تو اس کا شمار کب سے ہوگا، دعوئی کرنے اور معاملہ پیش کرنے کے وقت سے یا گم ہونے کے وقت سے یا حاکم (شرعی) کے فیصلہ کرنے کے بعد سے؟
(۳) کیا مفقود (لاپتہ شخص) کی بیوی فسخ نکاح میں خود مختار ہے یا اس کے لیے قاضی کا فیصلہ ہونے کی شرط ہے؟ اور نکاح فسخ ہونے کی کیا صورت ہو سکتی ہے۔
(۴) اگر قاضی کا فیصلہ شرط ہے تو کیا قاضی پر بھی یہ لازم ہے کہ پہلے مفقود کی خود تفتیش اور تلاش کرے جب اس کو نا امیدی ہو جائے تو اُس وقت بیوی کو کوئی مہلت وغیرہ دے یا عورت اور اس کے اولیاء کو تلاش کر لینا کافی ہے؟
(۵) جن علاقوں میں شرعی قاضی موجود نہیں جیسے ہندوستان وغیرہ تو وہاں پر اس کی کیا صورت ہے؟
(۶) مفقود کا حکم دار الحرب اور دارالسلام میں برابر ہے یا مختلف؟ اگر مختلف ہے تو پھر ہندوستان جیسے ممالک جن میں کروڑوں مسلمان آباد ہیں وہ دارالاسلام سمجھے جائیں گے۔
والسلام
نوٹ: فسخ نکاح سے اس جگہ فسخ اصطلاحی مراد نہیں۔ بلکہ محاورات اردو کے موافق فسخ کا لفظ اختیار کیا گیا۔ اور بغرض تفہیم عوام اس رسالہ میں اکثر مواقع میں لفظ فسخ ہی کا اطلاق کیا گیا ہے۔
And the complete explanation of the issue and all its conditions and stipulations could only be known from the Maliki scholars. For this reason, sensing this need, a detailed question was sent to the respected Muftis of the Maliki school in Madinah Munawwarah (may Allah increase it in honor and veneration). Many research scholars there wrote answers with great detail and clarity. But some doubts remained and some new questions arose, so those respected Muftis were troubled to write answers again. After the second answers, some further questions were needed, so for the third time questions were sent to those honorable personalities and answers were taken. All this correspondence is preserved in Madrasah Imdad al-Ulum, Thana Bhawan, and the collection of these fatawa has been included at the end of this book. In those fatawa, wherever a phrase provides the answer to our questions, numbers have been placed on all those words. In the following answers, we have sufficed with referencing those phrases because the common people do not need Arabic expressions, and the learned can themselves review the evidential phrases at the end of the treatise by referring to these numbers. Now the questions and answers are presented here in Urdu.
What do the Maliki scholars say? Request to the respected Maliki Muftis: question sheet regarding the following issues:
(1) A person who is mafqud al-khabar (missing) and after investigation and inquiry it is not known about him whether he is alive or dead. Does the wife of this person have the right that she, by some method, remove herself from that person's marriage and marry another? If she has this right, then does this woman need to wait some time, or will she be given authorization without a waiting period?
(3) If such a woman is given a waiting period, from when will it be counted: from the time of making a claim and presenting the matter, or from the time of disappearance, or after the ruling of the (shar‘i) judge?
(3) Is the wife of the missing (person) independent in annulling the marriage, or is a judge's ruling a condition for her? And what form can the annulment of marriage take?
(4) If the judge's ruling is a condition, then is it also necessary upon the judge that he himself first investigate and search for the missing person; when he becomes hopeless, then give the wife some waiting period etc., or is it sufficient that the woman and her guardians search?
(5) In regions where there is no shar‘i judge, like India etc., what is the procedure there?
(6) Is the ruling of the missing person the same in dar al-harb and dar al-Islam or different? If different, then will countries like India where millions of Muslims reside be considered dar al-Islam?
Wa al-salam.
Note: By 'annulment of marriage' here, technical faskh is not intended. Rather, in accordance with Urdu idiom, the word faskh has been chosen. And for the understanding of the common people, in most places in this treatise, the word faskh is used.
Source pages p. 107

Commentary
This passage is the introduction to a set of questions sent by the author, Ashraf Ali Thanwi, to Maliki scholars in Medina. The issue at hand is the wife of a person who has gone missing (mafqud al-khabar) and her right to remarry. The author notes that a complete clarification of the issue and its conditions could only be obtained from the Maliki school, implying that the Hanafi school's position did not provide a convenient solution for the common people. Thus, this is part of a legal stratagem (hila) by adopting a ruling from another madhhab. The correspondence went through three rounds to obtain satisfactory answers. The answers are included at the end of the book, referenced by numbers. The passage then lists six questions (though numbering has a typo: 1, then two 3's, then 4,5,6, but the content seems to cover all 6). The first question asks if the wife can end the marriage and remarry, and if a waiting period is needed. The second asks the starting point of any waiting period. The third asks if the wife is independent or requires a judge's ruling. The fourth asks if the judge must search himself. The fifth asks the procedure where no shar‘i judge exists. The sixth asks about the status of dar al-Islam vs. dar al-harb. A note clarifies that the term 'faskh' is used colloquially, not in the technical sense, for the benefit of common readers.

Key Terms
مفقود الخبر (mafqud al-khabar): A person whose whereabouts are unknown; it is not known whether he is alive or dead.
فسخ (faskh): Annulment; in this text used colloquially to mean dissolution of marriage, not necessarily the technical faskh (annulment) of Hanafi fiqh.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the main purpose of sending the six questions to Maliki scholars?
A) To challenge the validity of the Maliki madhhab
B) To obtain a hila (legal stratagem) for the wife of a missing person to remarry
C) To study comparative fiqh for academic purposes
D) To get a fatwa on inheritance of the missing person

Key Takeaway
When Hanafi law provides no solution for a missing person's wife, a mufti may adopt the Maliki ruling as a hila after thorough investigation and a waiting period.

Ruling on Missing Husband's Wife in Dar al-Harb
Maliki fatwa on mafqud's wife in Dar al-Harb and judge's duties

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and apply the conditions for the wife of a missing person to remarry in Dar al-Harb, including the judge's role and the start of the waiting period, using the Hanafi fatwa hierarchy.

Core Idea
The wife cannot remarry until the judge exhausts all searches and despairs; the waiting period starts from the judge's despair, not the disappearance.

This lesson examines the delicate case of a missing husband (mafqud) in non-Muslim territory (Dar al-Harb). Drawing from a Maliki fatwa referenced in a Hanafi work on legal stratagems, we unpack the procedural safeguards that prevent premature dissolution of marriage. The passage emphasizes the judge's investigative duty and the wife's inability to act without a verdict, illustrating how fatwa authority is determined across madhhabs.

Guided Path
1
Identify the central issue
Recognize that the problem is whether the wife of a mafqud in Dar al-Harb can ever remarry, and under what conditions. Note that the default position (Hanafi and Maliki) is that she cannot while the husband's peers are alive.
This establishes the baseline ruling.
2
Analyze the dispute on waiting period
Some Maliki scholars set fixed ages (70-75 years) based on natural lifespan. The author recommends deferring to experts. Understand that this conflict affects when the judge may permit remarriage.
The dispute is within the Maliki school, not between madhhabs.
3
Examine Ashhab's exception
Ashhab holds that Dar al-Harb shares the same ruling as Dar al-Islam: the wife cannot remarry until death is confirmed or an unreasonably long time passes. This is the preferred view according to the author.
Ashhab's view aligns more closely with the Hanafi position.
4
Extract procedural conditions for the judge
The judge must (a) conduct an active search in likely locations, (b) begin the four-year waiting period only after he despairs of finding the husband, and (c) not rely solely on the wife's or guardian's statements. The wife's release requires a court verdict.
These conditions ensure diligence and protect the marriage.
5
Apply the Hanafi fatwa hierarchy
Since this is a cross-madhhab reference, identify which position is mufta bihi in the Hanafi school. The author's preference for Ashhab suggests that the operative ruling is the stricter one: no remarriage without confirmed death or extreme passage of time.
The hierarchy: earlier texts (Quduri, Hidayah) set four years from judge's despair, but the Maliki discussion here confirms the procedural safeguards.

یہ فیصلہ تو دارالاسلام میں تھا اور دارالحرب میں مفقود کی بیوی کا حکم میں معقد کی ہو کا جو مالیہ کے نزدیک تو وہ ہی حکم ہے جو کہ حنفیہ کے نزدیک ہے یعنی جب تک اس کے ہم عمروگ حکم زندہ ہیں اُس وقت تک اُس کی بیوی کے لیے اُس کے نکاح سے علیحدہ ہونے اور دوسرا نکاح کرنے کی کوئی صورت نہیں جیسا کہ علامہ سعید بن صدیق حمفتی مذہب مالکی مدینہ منورہ کی تیسری روایت سے ظاہر ہوتا ہے ۔ اور بعض حضرات نے اس کی مدت عمر طبیعی کے اعتبار سے متعین بھی فرمادی ہے جس میں مختلف اقوال ہیں۔ خفیہ کے نزدیک مفقود کی بیوی کے لیے مدت انتظار بعض حضرات کے نزدیک [؟] سال، بعض کے نزدیک ۷۵ سال اور بعض کے نزدیک ستر سال وغیر ذلک لیکن بہتر یہ ہے کہ اس معاملہ کو اہلِ علم کے سپرد کر دیا جائے وہ حضرات تحقیق فرما کر اس کے سقم کا پتہ لگائیں ۔ لیکن اشہب نے (جو کہ حضرت امام مالک کے ممتاز شاگردوں میں ہیں اور فقہا ء مالکیہ میں بلند مقام رکھتے ہیں ) دارالحرب میں بھی مفقود کی بیوی کا وہ ہی حکم رکھا ہے جو کہ دارالاسلام میں گذر چکا ۔ کما ذكره ابن رشد في مقدماته (مدونه صفحه ۱۵۷ جلد۲) بحث قال واما المفقود في بلاد العرب نحكو حكم الاسير لا تزوج امرأته ولا يقم مال حتى يعلو موته او يأتي عليه من الزمان ما لا يجي الى مثله في قوله اصحابنا كلهو حاشا شعب فانه حكوله بحكو المفقود في المال والزوجة جمعياً الخ قاضی کے مایوس ہونے کے بعد سے مدت لگے گی اور دوسرے خط کشیدہ عبارت حضرت کی عربی عبارت کا ترجمہ ہے۔ (قاسمی) : سوال کا جواب یہ ہے کہ حاکم جو چار سال کی مدت انتظار کے لیے مقرر کرے گا۔ اس کی ابتداء اُس وقت سے کی جائے گی جس وقت حاکم خود بھی تفتیش کر کے پتہ ملنے سے نا اُمید ہو جائے اور قاضی کی عدالت میں پہنچنے سے قبل اور اُس کی تفتیش سے قبل خود کتنی ہی مدت گزر گئی ہو اس کا اعتبار نہیں ہے۔ کما فی اول الفتوى من العلامة سعيد بن صديق المالكي ويؤيده با وضع وجه ما في الرواية العشرين من العلامة الموصرف. تیسرے سوال کا جواب مفقود کی بیوی کےلیے بھی قاضی کا فیصلہ شرط ہے یہ ہے کہ رگم شد شخص مفقود کی بیوی کسی صورت میں اُس کے نکاح سے خارج ہونے میں خود تھار نہیں بلکہ ہر ایک حالت میں قامتی کا فیصلہ ہونا شرط ہے (جیسا کہ حضرت امام مالک رحمتہ اللہ علیہ سے روایت کی ہوئی روایت نہ میں مذکور ہے اور مقدمہ دائر کرنے کی صورت اور فتح نکاح کی صورت پہلے سوال کے شروع میں گذر چکی ۔ قاضی کے لیے مفقود کی تلاش کی تاکید چوتھے سوال کا جواب یہ ہے کہ تاضی پر بھی ضروری ہے کہ صرف عورت اور اُس کے ولی کی تفتیش اور اُن کے بیان پر اکتفا نہ کرے بلکہ خود بھی تلاش کرائے اور تلاش کرنے کی صورت یہ ہے کہ قاضی اور حاکم کو جس جس جگہ مفقود لاپتہ شخص) کے چلے جانے کا گمان غالب ہو اُس اُس جگہ آدمی بھیجا جائے لیے ہو كما في شرح الدردير (صفحه ۳۹۹ جلد ) من حين العجز عن خبره بالبحث عند في الأماكن التي يظن ذهابه اليها من البلدان بان يرسل الحاكورسولا بكتاب الحاكو تلك الاماكن مشتمل على صفة الرجل وحرفة نے یہ تیسرا قول ہماری رائے میں سب سے زیادہ بہتر ہے۔
This ruling was in Dar al-Islam, and in Dar al-Harb, the ruling for the wife of a missing person according to the Maliki school is the same as that of the Hanafis: as long as his peers are alive, there is no way for his wife to separate from her marriage and marry another, as is apparent from the third narration of Allamah Sa'id bin Siddiq al-Maliki of Madinah. Some have determined a period based on natural lifespan, with various opinions. According to the Malikis, the waiting period for the missing person's wife is: according to some, [?] years; according to some, 75 years; according to some, 70 years; etc. But it is better to entrust this matter to scholars who can investigate and determine its flaw. However, Ashhab (who is among Imam Malik's distinguished students and holds a high rank in Maliki fiqh) gave the same ruling for the missing person's wife in Dar al-Harb as in Dar al-Islam. As mentioned by Ibn Rushd in his Muqaddimat (Madunah, vol. 2, p. 157): 'He said: As for the missing person in enemy lands, we apply the ruling of a captive: his wife is not married, and his wealth is not distributed until his death is known or such time passes that he cannot be expected to return from life.' This is the opinion of all our companions except Ashhab, who applied the ruling of the missing person to both wealth and wife. The judge will count the period from when he despairs. The underlined part is a translation of the Arabic passage. (Qasimi) Answer to the question: The waiting period of four years that the ruler (hakim) will set will begin from the time when the ruler himself, after investigation, becomes hopeless of finding [the missing person]. Any time that passed before the case reached the judge's court and before his investigation is not considered. As in the first fatwa of Allamah Sa'id bin Siddiq al-Maliki, and it is supported by the twentieth narration of Allamah al-Musarraf. Answer to the third question: For the missing person's wife, the judge's verdict is also a condition. That is, the missing person's wife cannot free herself from the marriage in any way without a judge's verdict. (As mentioned in the narration from Imam Malik, and the procedure for filing the case and dissolving the marriage was mentioned at the beginning of the first question.) Emphasis on the judge's search. Answer to the fourth question: It is also necessary for the judge not to rely solely on the investigation of the woman and her guardian and their statement, but he himself should search. The method of search is that the judge and ruler should send men to every place where there is a strong presumption that the missing person may have gone, as in Sharh al-Dardir (vol. 399): 'From the time of inability to hear news of him, by searching in the places where he is thought to have gone among the cities, by the ruler sending a messenger with a letter to the rulers of those places, containing the description and profession of the man.' This third opinion is, in our view, the best.
Source pages p. 111

Commentary
This passage is a fatwa response addressing the complex case of a missing husband (mafqud) in Dar al-Harb (non-Muslim territory) according to the Maliki school, but referenced in a Hanafi work on legal stratagems. It opens by stating that in Dar al-Harb, the Maliki ruling is similar to the Hanafi ruling: the wife cannot remarry as long as the husband's peers are alive (i.e., until natural life expectancy ends). Some scholars set specific durations (e.g., 70, 75 years) based on natural lifespan, but the author recommends deferring to experts. Then it notes an exception: the Maliki scholar Ashhab rules identically for Dar al-Harb and Dar al-Islam, treating the missing person like a captive whose wife cannot remarry until death is confirmed or a very long time passes. This is supported by Ibn Rushd. On page 112, the passage addresses three sub-questions: (1) The beginning of the four-year waiting period fixed by the judge: it starts from when the judge himself despairs after investigation, not from the earlier disappearance. (2) The necessity of the judge's verdict: the wife cannot dissolve the marriage without a court order. (3) The judge's duty to actively search beyond the woman's claim, sending investigators to places the husband might have gone, as per Sharh al-Dardir. The author concludes that the third opinion (Ashhab's) is best. This illustrates the procedural rigor required in such delicate family law matters.

Key Terms
مفقود (mafqud): A person whose whereabouts are unknown and whose life or death is uncertain.
دار الحرب (dar al-harb): Territory not under Muslim rule, where the missing person may be in enemy lands.
قاضی (qadi): A judge who has the authority to issue verdicts regarding marital dissolution in cases of missing persons.
مدت انتظار (muddat intizar): The waiting period for the wife, during which she cannot remarry until the judge's ruling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) From the date the judge becomes hopeless after investigation.
C) From the date the wife files a case.
D) From the date the husband's peers die.

Key Takeaway
The wife of a missing person cannot remarry without a judge's verdict, and the waiting period only starts after the judge exhausts all reasonable search and despairs of finding the husband.

The Start of the Waiting Period for the Missing Person's Wife
Clarifies the start of the waiting period and the qadi's duty to search

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state when the four-year waiting period begins for the wife of a missing person and explain the qadi's obligation to conduct a thorough search before declaring hopelessness.

Core Idea
The waiting period only starts after the qadi's own investigation yields no hope.

In Hanafi fatwa practice, the missing person's wife cannot simply count time from disappearance. The qadi must actively search until hopelessness. This lesson clarifies the moment the waiting period begins and the scope of the qadi's duty.

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition for the start of the waiting period
Find the exact phrase in the text that describes when the four-year period begins. Look for the ruler's hopelessness after investigation.
2
Recognize prior elapsed time is irrelevant
The text explicitly states that any period before the qadi's court and investigation is not counted. This is a key rule.
This prevents the wife from claiming time before the case was filed.
3
Understand the qadi's investigation duty
The qadi must not rely only on the wife or guardian's statements. He must personally arrange a search.
The method includes sending agents, letters, and using newspapers.
4
Apply the rule to a scenario
Given a timeline of disappearance, filing, and qadi's investigation, determine when the waiting period begins.
Use the text: only after the qadi becomes hopeless.

وجه ما في الرواية العشرين من العلامة الموصرف.

نے یہ تیسرا قول ہماری رائے میں سب سے زیادہ بہتر ہے۔
نسبه ليفتش عنه فيها اهـ .
اور جس جگہ صرف احتمال ہو وہاں اگر خط کو کافی سمجھے تو وہاں پر خطوط بھیج کو تحقیق کی جائے اور اگر اخبار میں شائع کرنے سے خبر ملنے دیا اس کے بارے میں کچھ امید ہو تو یہ صورت بھی اختیار کی جائے ۔
for
خلاصہ یہ کہ تحقیق و تفتیش میں پوری کوشش کی جائے اور جب اُس کی تلاش سے قطعی طور پر مایوسی ہو جائے اُس وقت مذکورۃ الصدر طریقہ پر چار سال کے مزید انتظار کا حکم کیا جائے جیسا کہ علامہ صدیق مفتی مالکیہ، رینہ
منورہ کی بیسویں روایت میں ہے۔
مفقود کی تحقیق مفقود کی تنقید کے اخراجات کا کون ذمہ دار ہے ؟ اور تفتیش کے تمام اخراجات کے سلسلہ میں فقہاء میں اختلاف ہے بعض حضرات نے فرمایا یہ تمام اخراجات (مقدمہ دائر کرنے والی) عورت (مفقود کی بیوی ) کے ذمہ ہے اور بعض نے فرمایا بیت المال کے ذمہ ہے اور بعض کے نزدیک یہ تفصیل ہے کہ اگر بیوی کے پاس مال ہو تو تحقیق و تفتیش کے تمام اخراجات اُس کے ذمہ ہوں گے ورنہ بیت المال کے ذمہ جیسا کہ حضرت علا مارینا باشم کے فتوی میں نے روایت ہے
اور جس جگہ بیت المال نہ ہو جیسے ہندوستان وغیرہ میں اگر ان مواقع میں حکومت اخراجات برداشت کر سے تو بہتر ہے ورنہ مسلمانوں سے چندہ کروا لیا جائے۔
پانچویں سوال کا جواب یہ ہے کہ
قاضی کے فیصد کے قائم مقام حضرات جن ملکوں میں شرعی قاضی موجود نہیں
This is that the ruler who will prescribe the four-year waiting period — its beginning will be from the time when the ruler himself, after investigation, becomes hopeless of finding news; and any period that passed before reaching the qadi's court and before his investigation is not considered. As in the first fatwa of Allamah Sa'id bin Siddiq al-Maliki, supported by what is in the twentieth narration of Allamah al-Mawsiri. Answer to the third question: The decision of the qadi is also a condition for the wife of the missing person. That is, the wife of the missing person is not herself empowered to exit from his marriage in any way; rather, in every situation, the qadi's decision is a condition. (As mentioned in the narration from Imam Malik, may Allah have mercy on him, and the procedure for filing a case and opening marriage has already passed at the beginning of the first question.) Emphasizing the search for the missing person. Answer to the fourth question: It is also necessary upon the qadi that he does not rely only on the investigation and statements of the woman and her guardian, but he himself should arrange a search. The manner of searching is that the qadi and ruler should send a person to every place where there is a strong presumption that the missing person (lost person) went, as in Sharh al-Dardir (page 399, volume) from the time of inability to obtain news by searching in the places where he is thought to have gone from the cities, by sending the ruler a messenger with a book of the ruler to those places containing the description of the man and his profession. This third opinion is, in our view, the best. His lineage to search therein, etc. And for places where there is only probability, if he deems a letter sufficient, then let letters be sent for investigation. And if there is hope of getting news by publishing in newspapers, this method should also be adopted. Summary: full effort should be made in research and investigation, and when there is absolute despair of finding him, then according to the above-mentioned method, the order of waiting four more years should be given, as is in the twentieth narration of Allamah Siddiq Mufti Maliki. Who is responsible for the expenses of researching and investigating the missing person? There is disagreement among jurists regarding all expenses of investigation. Some have said that all these expenses are upon the woman (the missing person's wife) (the one filing the case). Some said it is upon the public treasury. And some are of the detail that if the wife has wealth, then all expenses of research and investigation are upon her; otherwise upon the public treasury, as narrated in the fatwa of Hadrat Allamah al-Marini Bashm. And in places where there is no public treasury, like India etc., if in such situations the government bears the expenses, it is better; otherwise, let a collection be made from the Muslims. Answer to the fifth question: The deputies of the qadi, in countries where there is no shari qadi...
Source pages p. 112

Commentary
This passage is a series of rulings (fatwa) regarding the procedure for the wife of a missing person (mafqud) to remarry. The text addresses five main points. First, the four-year waiting period imposed by the ruler (hakim) only begins when the ruler himself, after active investigation, becomes hopeless of finding any news. Any elapsed time before the case reaches the qadi is disregarded. Second, the qadi's verdict is absolutely necessary; the wife cannot independently declare herself free from the marriage. Third, the qadi must conduct a thorough search, not relying solely on the wife's or her guardian's statements. He should send agents to places where the missing person might have gone, as detailed in Sharh al-Dardir. If only probability exists, letters may suffice, and newspapers can be used. Fourth, regarding expenses, there is ikhtilaf: some say the wife pays, others say the public treasury (bayt al-mal), and a third view distinguishes based on the wife's wealth. In the absence of bayt al-mal, the government should pay or a collection from Muslims. Fifth, the text begins discussing the deputies of the qadi in areas without a shari qadi, indicating continuation.

Key Terms
مفقود (mafqud): A person whose whereabouts are unknown and whose life or death is uncertain.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, when does the four-year waiting period for the missing person's wife begin?
A) Upon the disappearance of the husband
B) When the wife files a case with the qadi
C) When the qadi becomes hopeless after investigation
D) After four years of absence have passed

Key Takeaway
The waiting period for the missing person's wife only starts after the qadi's own investigation yields no hope; prior time is disregarded.

Who Bears the Expenses of Investigating the Missing Person?
Presents a disagreement on expenses for investigating a missing person

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three opinions on who bears the expenses of searching for a missing person and state the preferred Hanafi view per the passage.

Core Idea
Expenses of investigating a missing person are either on the wife, the public treasury, or depend on the wife's wealth.

When a missing person must be searched, who pays? Hanafi jurists differed. This lesson examines three opinions and the author's preferred view, with practical application in modern contexts without bayt al-mal.

Guided Path
1
List the three opinions on expenses
Identify each opinion from the text: (1) wife pays, (2) bayt al-mal pays, (3) detail based on wife's wealth.
The third opinion is the author's preference.
2
Identify the basis for each opinion
The text does not give detailed evidence, but we can infer the rationale: plaintiff bears costs vs. public benefit vs. capacity.
3
Note the author's tarjih
The author says the third opinion is the best (بِہتر). This is the mufta bihi.
4
Apply to modern scenario without bayt al-mal
The text addresses India where no bayt al-mal exists: government should pay or collection from Muslims.
This is a practical extension of the detailed view.
5
Formulate a fatwa
Given a specific case (rich wife, poor wife, etc.), determine who pays based on the preferred opinion and local conditions.

Jurisdiction for the Wife of a Missing Husband
Ruling on jurisdiction when the wife moves to a place with a qadi

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rule that a qadi's jurisdiction is established by the plaintiff's presence, not the defendant's, in cases involving a missing husband.

Core Idea
A qadi's decision is valid for the wife of a missing husband when she moves to his territory, even if the husband is absent.

In the previous lesson, we discussed the law regarding a missing husband (mafqud). This appendix (tamimah) addresses a practical concern: where should the wife file her case? The answer is that she may go to any place where a shari'ah judge or Muslim ruler exists, and his ruling will be effective. This lesson explains the reasoning behind this procedural rule.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Rule
The passage states: the qadi's decision is sufficient (kafi) for the wife of a missing husband when she files a case in his territory.
2
Identify the Condition
The condition is that the wife moves to a place where a shari'ah judge (qadi) or Muslim ruler (musliman hakim) is present and she files a case with him.
The text uses 'or' indicating either authority suffices.
3
Extract the Talil (Legal Reason)
The reason given is that the wife, by being in that locality, enters the qadi's jurisdiction (wilayah), and it is not required that the missing husband also be under that jurisdiction.
The talil is explicit: 'because when the qadi is located in such a woman's area, that woman enters into the qadi's jurisdiction, and jurisdiction is not a condition for the missing person.'
4
Identify the Implied Principle
The general rule of territorial jurisdiction is relaxed in cases of missing persons to prevent hardship.
This is consistent with the qawida: 'al-mashaqqah tajlib al-taysir'.
5
Apply to Similar Cases
This ruling can be extended to any case where the defendant is missing, such as inheritance claims or divorce petitions, as long as the plaintiff is present and the authority is competent.
The mufta bihi position accepts this as a valid hila (legal device).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تتمه هذ الجواب اگر مفقود کی بیوی ایسی جگہ چلی جائے کہ جہاں پر شرعی قاضی یا مسلمان حاکم موجود ہو اور اس کے پاس مقدمہ دائر کرے تو اس کا فیصلہ بھی مفقود کی بیوی کے لیے کافی ہے کیونکہ جب قاضی ایسی مذکورہ عورت کے علاقہ میں چلا جائے تو وہ عورت قاضی کی ولایت میں داخل ہو جائے گی اور مفقود کے لیے ولایت شرط نہیں ہے۔ جیسا کہ ظاہر ہے۔
Appendix to this answer: If the wife of the missing person goes to a place where a shari'ah judge or Muslim ruler is present and she files a case with him, then his decision is also sufficient for the wife of the missing person, because when the qadi is located in such a woman's area, that woman enters into the qadi's jurisdiction, and jurisdiction is not a condition for the missing person. As is apparent.
Source pages p. 114

Commentary
This appendix (tamimah) clarifies a procedural point: if the wife of a missing husband moves to a locality where a shari'ah judge or Muslim ruler exists and she files her case there, the judge's ruling is valid and sufficient for her. The reason given is that by being in that locality, the wife comes under the qadi's jurisdiction (wilayah), and it is not required that the husband also be under that jurisdiction. This is a practical rule because the husband is missing and his location is unknown. It facilitates the wife's access to a competent authority. This ruling is part of the hila for the missing husband case.

Key Terms
ولایت (wilayah): legal jurisdiction; the authority of a qadi over persons and cases within his territory

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because the husband also moves
B) Because the wife enters the qadi's jurisdiction
C) Because the qadi has universal authority
D) Because the husband is presumed dead

Key Takeaway
For the wife of a missing husband, the qadi's jurisdiction is established by her presence, not her husband's.

The Hila for the Wife of a Missing Husband in Non-Muslim Lands
Hila for wife of missing husband in dar al-harb.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct a fatwa applicable to the wife of a missing husband in non-Muslim lands, applying the conditions of the Maliki hila and the Hanafi framework for fatwa.

Core Idea
When no qadhi is available, a committee of Muslims may issue a ruling equivalent to a qadhi's, following Maliki school after four years.

This passage provides a practical solution (hila) for a pressing family law problem: the wife of a missing husband in lands without an Islamic judge. Ashraf Ali Thanwi explains how to proceed when a Sharia qadhi is absent, drawing on the Maliki school to allow remarriage after four years. This lesson examines the conditions and legal basis of this hila, and how a mufti can rely upon it.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and legal issue
The wife of a man whose whereabouts are unknown (mafqud al-khabr) seeks to remarry. The jurisdiction lacks a Sharia qadhi. Determine the primary obstacle: absence of a qadhi who can rule on dissolving the marriage.
The Hanafi school ordinarily requires a longer waiting period or certainty of death.
2
Assess alternatives to a qadhi
Check if a Muslim official or ruler with authority to decide Sharia cases exists. If yes, their ruling stands in place of a qadhi. If not, move to the next step.
The author refers to officials authorized by the government who are Muslim and rule by Sharia.
3
Form a Sharia committee
If no Muslim ruler or qadhi is available, organize a committee of pious Muslims (a 'Sharia committee') to investigate the case thoroughly, following conditions outlined in earlier parts of the text.
The committee's ruling is considered equivalent to a qadhi's if they follow the proper procedure.
4
Apply Maliki ruling on waiting period
According to the Maliki school, the wife may remarry after four years of waiting, provided a thorough investigation has been conducted. This is the hila (legal device) adopted by the author.
The Maliki position relies on the practice of Caliph Umar (RA) and is considered valid in dar al-harb.
5
Issue the fatwa
The mufti should state that the wife may remarry after four years, after forming a committee, observing 'iddah of death (four months and ten days), and completing the investigation. Clarify that this is a concession (rukhsah) due to necessity.
Ensure the fatwa mentions authority: adopted from Maliki school for darurah.

Source Text

شعائر تاحال قائم نہیں، ان تمام میں مفقود کا حکم وہی ہے جو کہ دارالاسلام میں ہے۔ بلکہ جس دار الحرب میں اسلامی شعائر بھی موجود نہ ہوں اگر وہاں پر مسلمانوں کو صلح وغیرہ کی وجہ سے آنا جانا اور تفتیش کرنا مسئلہ پر کوئی اثر نہیں پڑے گا اور مفقود کی بیوی کو ان ملکوں میں چار سال کے بعد عدت وفات گزار کر نکاح ثانی کا اختیار دیا جائے گا جیسا کہ علامہ الفا باشیم کی پانچویں روایت اور علامہ طیب کی ۲۵ ویں روایت Just as the condition of all cities of India except Islamic states is, then there those officials who have authority from the government to decide such matters, if they are Muslims and decide according to Sharia, their decision also stands in place of a qadhi's decision, as has passed. What to do if a Muslim ruler is not available? If a Muslim ruler is not present or from his court a decision according to Sharia law is not possible, then according to the Maliki school, a group of pious Muslims should be formed, i.e., a Sharia committee, and they should investigate according to the above description, and after complete investigation give a decision. Then this decision will also be at the level of a qadhi's decision, but it is necessary that it be in accordance with the conditions of investigation that have passed. Completion of this answer: If the wife of a missing person goes to a place where a Sharia qadhi or Muslim ruler is present and files a case with him, then his decision is sufficient for the wife of the missing person, because when the qadhi comes to the area of that woman, she enters under the authority of the qadhi, and for the missing person, authority is not a condition, as is apparent. But in the old copy, there was a detailed footnote at this place which could be considered a separate article, so that footnote is presented as a separate article on the next page. However, if the wife of an insane or impotent man goes alone to the area of a qadhi, the qadhi's decision will not be valid; rather it is necessary that the insane man and the impotent man also be in the area of that qadhi. The answer to the question is that the ruling of dar al-harb and India is different from dar al-Islam, as has passed in detail in the answer to the first question. But from the fatwas of Maliki scholars, it appears that in all those countries like India, Egypt, Syria, etc., where despite infidel rule Islamic rites are not yet established, the ruling of the missing person is the same as in dar al-Islam. Rather, even in that dar al-harb where Islamic rites are not present, if it is possible for Muslims to come and go and investigate due to peace treaties, the ruling of the missing person is the same as in dar al-Islam. So the fundamental basis is extraordinary investigation and search for the missing person. Therefore, the difference of scholars regarding India being dar al-harb has no effect on this issue, and the wife of the missing person in those countries will be given the option to remarry after four years having observed the waiting period of death, as is found in the fifth narration of Allamah Alfa Bashim and the 25th narration of Allamah Tayyib. (The issue of guardianship of the missing husband: content of the footnote of the old copy) If at this point someone doubts that the missing person is a resident of a particular place? The narration adopted from the Maliki school: As for the second question, which is: Is the decision of the judge or the group of Muslims binding regarding waiting four years, or does she announce without a ruling from the rulers? Commentary
This passage presents a legal stratagem (hila) for the wife of a missing husband (mafqud) in non-Muslim lands where a proper Islamic qadhi is unavailable. The author, Ashraf Ali Thanwi, explains that in such circumstances, if a Muslim official from the government can adjudicate according to Sharia, his ruling is valid. If no such official exists, a committee of reliable Muslims can be formed, following the Maliki school, to conduct a thorough investigation and issue a ruling equivalent to that of a qadhi. The wife may also travel to a place where a qadhi is present, and his ruling will be binding due to her entering his jurisdiction. However, this does not apply to the wife of an insane or impotent husband, where the husband must also be in the qadhi's jurisdiction. The author further clarifies that despite differences among scholars about whether India is dar al-harb or dar al-Islam, the key is the possibility of investigation. Citing Maliki fatwas, the wife may remarry after four years of waiting (based on the Maliki school). This is a practical solution (hila) adopted from the Maliki school to resolve the wife's hardship.

Key Terms
مفقود الخبر (mafqud al-khabr): A person whose whereabouts are unknown and no news has been received for an extended period.
دار الحرب (dar al-harb): Territory under non-Muslim rule where Islamic law is not enforced by the state.
حيلة شرعية (hila shar'iyyah): A legal stratagem or device that achieves a permissible outcome while complying with Sharia, often used to avoid hardship.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) The committee must consist of reliable Muslims and conduct a thorough investigation according to the conditions mentioned earlier.
C) The committee must include at least one qadhi.
D) The committee's ruling must be approved by a Maliki scholar.

Key Takeaway
The wife of a missing husband in non-Muslim lands may remarry after four years through a Maliki-based hila, provided a thorough investigation by a committee of Muslims.

Minor's Self-Marriage Without Guardian: Jurisdictional Condition
Rasm rule with territorial condition for guardian authority

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on a minor's self-marriage without a guardian in the absence of a ruler and identify the jurisdictional condition that determines whether the marriage is suspended or void.

Core Idea
A minor's self-marriage without a guardian is suspended until puberty, but only if the place is under the sultan's authority; otherwise it is void.

This passage from Radd al-Muhtar addresses a nuanced scenario: a minor girl marries herself without a guardian in a location where no qadi is present. The ruling hinges on whether that location falls under the jurisdiction (wilayah) of the sultan. This lesson unpacks the condition of wilayah and its implications for the validity of the marriage, guiding muftis in assessing similar cases in Muslim-minority contexts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base ruling
Find the initial ruling: when a minor marries herself without a guardian and no ruler is present, the marriage is suspended (tawaqquf) until she ratifies it after puberty.
The suspension is not nullity; it gives the minor a choice.
2
Locate the condition for suspension
Note that the suspension is conditioned on the place of contract being under the authority of the sultan, even if no specific qadi is appointed there.
The phrase 'i.e., in the place of contract' clarifies that the relevant condition is the location's jurisdiction.
3
Apply the condition to different territories
The passage states that if the contract occurs in dar al-harb, desert, sea, or similar areas outside any Muslim authority, the marriage becomes void immediately because no one can ratify it.
Examples like dar al-harb highlight that the rule is general: even minors originally from dar al-islam lose the option if they are in dar al-harb.
4
Contrast with the Maliki view
The Hanafi position is reinforced by a Maliki quote that similarly conditions the qadi's authority on the woman being within his jurisdiction, emphasizing the importance of territorial wilayah.
This cross-madhhab reference supports the principle that jurisdiction is a prerequisite for guardian authority.
5
Formulate the rasm al-mufti principle
Synthesize: For a marriage to be validly suspended (or void if no authority), the mufti must ascertain whether the place of contract falls under any recognized Islamic jurisdiction, either by a sultan, qadi, or equivalent authority.
This principle helps muftis in modern contexts where Islamic governments may not be present.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
رو المختار جلد ۲ باب الولی ہیں ہے : " وصغيرة زوجت نفسها ولا ولى وان حاكو تمه توقف ونفذ با جان ها بعد بلو عنها لان له مميزاً وهو السلطان. قوله ولا حاكو شمه اى فى موضع العقد - قوله توقف هذا مبنى على كفاية ذالك المكان تحت ولايته السلطان وان لم يكن تحت ولاية قاضي وعليه فبطلات العقد يتصور فيما اذا كان في دار العرب او البحر او المفازة ونحو ذلك بغلاف القرى والامصار ويدل عليه ما في العقد في فصل الوكالة بالنكاح حيث قال وما لا يجيز له اى ما ليس له من يقدر على الاجازة يبطل كما اذا كانت تحتدحرة فزيجه الفضولي امة اواخت امرأته او خامسة وزوجة معقدة او مجنونة او صغيرة يتيمة في دار العزب او اذا العريكن سلطان ولا قاض لحد ممن يقدر على الامضاء حالة العقد فوقع بالملك اهـ
چونکہ اس روایت میں مجنونة او صغيرة في دار الحرب عام ہے اس کو کہ وہ میمتونہ یا صغیرہ اول ہی سے دار الحرب میں ہو یا پیشتر دارالاسلام میں تھی اور اب دارالوب میں چلی گئی اس عموم کی وجہ سے ثابت ہوا کہ ولایت، سابقہ کا اعتبار نہیں ورنہ اس مجنونہ اورضعیرہ میں جو دارالاسلام سے گئی ہو اختیار ہوتا۔ دنیز قول شامی ای فی موضع العقد أور ذلك المكان تحت ولاية السلطان کے لفظ سے واضح ہے کہ ولایت کے لیے سلطان وقامتی کے علاقہ میں ہونا شرط ہے ۔ اور مالکیہ نے تو اس کی بہت ہی صاف تصریح کی ہے۔ چنانچہ شرح دردیر میں ہے۔ (ولایزرج) القاضی (امرأة) اى لا يتولى عقد نكاحها حيث لا ولى لها الا الحاكم وليست بولاية)
بات كانت خارجة عنها اولا ولاية عليها وان كان اصلها من اهلها اه - صفحه ۹۶ جلد ۳) والله اعلم
From Radd al-Muhtar, Volume 2, Chapter of Guardianship: 'A minor girl married herself, and there was no guardian, and if there was no ruler [to approve], it is suspended and becomes effective by her permission after puberty, because there is someone who can approve, namely the sultan.' His saying: 'and if there is no ruler' means in the place of contract. His saying: 'suspended' is based on the sufficiency of that place being under the authority of the sultan, even if it is not under the authority of a qadi. Accordingly, the nullity of the contract is conceivable when it is in the land of Arabs, or sea, or desert, and the like, excluding towns and cities. And this is supported by what is in al-`Aqd in the chapter of agency in marriage where he said: 'And what cannot be ratified by him, i.e., what has no one who can ratify, becomes void.' For example, if a woman came forward and a stranger married her to a slave girl, or his wife's sister, or a fifth wife, or a wife in `iddah, or an insane woman, or a minor orphan in a land where there is no sultan and no qadi, none capable of execution at the time of contract, so the contract is void.' Because in this narration, 'insane' and 'minor' in dar al-harb are general, whether they were originally there or came from dar al-islam. Due to this generality, it is established that previous guardianship is not considered; otherwise, there would be a choice for the insane and minor who came from dar al-islam. And the expression of al-Shami: 'i.e., in the place of contract, or that place is under the authority of the sultan', shows that for guardianship, being in the territory of the sultan and qadi is a condition. The Malikis have explicitly stated this. Thus in Sharh al-Dardir: 'The qadi does not marry a woman (i.e., does not conduct her marriage contract) when she has no guardian except the ruler, and she is not under his jurisdiction if she was outside his territory, even if her origin is from his people.'
Source pages p. 117

Commentary
This passage from Radd al-Muhtar addresses the case of a minor girl who marries herself without a guardian in a place where there is no sultan or qadi. The ruling is that the marriage is suspended (tawaqquf) until she reaches puberty and ratifies it, because the sultan is the one who can approve. The condition is that the place of contract must be under the sultan's authority even if no specific qadi is there. If the place is outside any authority (like desert, sea, dar al-harb), then the marriage is void from the start. The text then discusses the generality of the rule: it applies whether the minor was originally in dar al-harb or moved there. This shows that current jurisdiction is necessary; past jurisdiction does not count. The passage concludes with a quote from the Maliki school in Sharh al-Dardir, which also states that the qadi cannot marry a woman who is outside his jurisdiction, even if her family is from his area. This reinforces the rasm al-mufti principle that jurisdiction is a condition for the validity of a marriage contract by a guardian or ruler.

Key Terms
صغيرة (saghīrah): A female minor who has not yet reached puberty.
حاكو (ḥākim): Ruler or judge with authority to approve marriage contracts.
توقف (tawaqquf): Suspension of a contract's effectiveness until a condition is met (here, ratification at puberty).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what happens to a minor girl's marriage contracted without a guardian in a place without a sultan or qadi?
A) It is immediately valid
B) It is completely void
C) It is suspended until she ratifies it at puberty
D) It is valid only if her father approves later

Key Takeaway
A minor's self-marriage without a guardian is suspended, not void, as long as the place of contract is under the sultan's authority; otherwise it is void from the start.

Ruling on Missing Person Returning After Death Decree: Hanafi vs Maliki Positions
Explains the ruling for a missing person returning after qadi declares death

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Hanafi and Maliki positions on the wife's fate when a missing husband returns after a death decree, and apply the condition of the second husband's knowledge as per the Maliki school.

Core Idea
When a missing husband returns, the wife always goes back to him in Hanafi; Maliki requires the second husband to be unaware of the missing status for her to stay.

This passage addresses a critical issue in family law: the legal consequences when a person declared dead later returns. The author presents two scenarios and compares the Hanafi and Maliki schools, highlighting a nuance in the Maliki position that depends on the second husband's knowledge. Understanding this ruling is essential for muftis dealing with modern cases of long-term disappearance.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios of return
The passage describes two scenarios: first, the missing husband returns before valid seclusion (khalwat sahiha) with the second husband; second, he returns after valid seclusion has occurred.
The timing relative to iddah and second marriage is also noted but the key distinction is occurrence of khalwat.
2
State the Hanafi position and its basis
Imam Abu Hanifa holds that the wife returns to the first husband in all circumstances, regardless of the second marriage or consummation, as clarified by Shams al-A'imma in al-Mabsut. The death decree is seen as procedural, not actually dissolving the marriage.
This is the mufta bihi position for Hanafi fatwa.
3
State the Maliki well-known position and its nuance
The well-known Maliki position is that after consummation, the wife stays with the second husband. However, a condition applies: if the second husband knew that the woman's husband was missing, then even after consummation, the wife returns to the first husband.
This nuance is attributed to a reliable Maliki source, but not widely mentioned.
4
Explain the importance of the second husband's knowledge
Knowledge of the missing status at the time of marriage affects the ruling. If the second husband knew, the marriage is considered invalid or the first marriage revives; if he did not know, the second marriage is protected.
This condition is critical for fatwa where facts of knowledge are disputed.
5
Derive the practical fatwa hierarchy
In Hanafi, the answer is absolute: wife to first husband. In Maliki, it depends on knowledge. For a mufti following Hanafi, the ruling is clear; but when advising a Maliki questioner, one must ask about the second husband's knowledge.
This shows the importance of knowing madhhab-specific conditions.

Source Text

لیکن اس مفقود کی موت کے حکم کے بعد اگر مفقود واپس آجائے؟ تلسلہ میں حضرت امام اعظم حضرت امام ابوحنیفہ کا مذہب یہ ہے کہ اگر مفقود، مردہ قرار دیے جانے کے بعد بھی واپس آجائے تو اُس کی بیوی ہر حالت میں اُسی کو ملے گی خواہ عدت وفات کے اندر آئے یا عدت گزرنے کے بعد اور خواہ دوسرا نکاح ہونے اور خلوت اور ہمبستری کے بعد آئے یا پہلے ؟ جیسا کہ حضرت شمس الائمہ نے مبسوط میں وضاحت کی ہے اور فرمایا ہے۔ ایک ضروری بات قابل تنبیہ یہ ہے کہ مالکیہ کے مذہب شہر میں بھی روح ثانی سے میتری کے بعد شوہر اول کا حق فوت ہو جانے کی ایک شرط ہے جس کا علمائے مدینہ کے قبادی میں تذکرہ نہیں ہے نہ علوم اس کا ذکر کس وجہ سے رہ گیا در ستان کی معتبر و مستند کتاب میں موجود ہے ۔ وہ شرط یہ ہے کہ دوسرے خاوند کو اس بات کی خبرنہ ہو کہ اس عورت کا خاوند لاپتہ ہے۔ اور اگر خبر ہو کہ اس کا خاوند لاپتہ ہے تو پھر شوہر ثانی کے دخول ومبستری کے بعد واپس آنے پر بھی شوہر اول کا نکاح باقی رکھا جائے گا اور اُسی کو مل جاوے گی۔ كذا صبح به في مختصر التحليل وشرحه العلامة الدردير الصفحه ٢٠٠ ج ( فتكون المفقود فيها اذاجاء اوتیں جان او موته في العدة او بعدة او بعدها وقبل عقد الثاني از بعده وقبل تلذذه بها او بعده عالما بما ذكر وتفوت عليه تكون الثاني ان تلذذبها خیر عالم اور پس مذہب مشہور کی بنا پر بھی صرف اُس جگہ مالکیہ کو اختلاف ہوگا جہاں شوہر نائی کو خبر نہ ہو کہ یہ زوجہ مفقود ہے دھو نا ز را امنہ جسے خان قال قائل اذار جع عشر عن مذهبه فكيف يسوم للمالكية القول بمذهبه السابق المرجوع عنه تلنا الرجوع مختلف فيه اى منهم الرجوع عنا الاحناف ولم يصيح عند المالكية كما قال ابن قدامة في كتابه المسمى بالمفتی صفحه ۱۳۲ جلد ۹) قال الانترسم قلت عقاد المالك) فروى من ربه ضعیف ان عشر قال فلان هذا قال لا الا ان يكذب انسان ١٣٥ منه
The ruling of the two scenarios of the missing person's return: The missing person for whom after filing a case or after investigation and waiting for four years, the qadi issued a death ruling. If after the death ruling he returns, there are two scenarios. First scenario: that he returns before valid seclusion (khalwat sahiha) with the second husband, whether after the death-iddah or within the death-iddah, and whether before the second marriage or after. Second scenario: that he returns at a time when after completing the death-iddah the woman has married another person and valid seclusion has also occurred. Among these, the ruling of the first scenario is unanimously that the wife will remain in the marriage of the first husband and cannot stay with the second husband, as is clear from the fatwa narrations 14 and 15 and narration 24 of Allamah Salih Maliki. And in the second scenario, the well-known Maliki school is that the wife will stay with the second husband; the first husband has no connection with her now, as mentioned in the fifteenth, seventh, and twentieth narrations of Allamah Tayyib bin Ishaq Rafi'i. Although Hadrat Sha'rani wrote in Mizan and another narration that in any case that woman will stay with the first husband. And from Imam Shafi'i, the preponderant opinion is that the second marriage is null, meaning when the missing person returns. However, after the death ruling of this missing person, if the missing person returns? In this series, the school of Imam A'zam Imam Abu Hanifa is that if the missing person returns even after being declared dead, his wife will go to him in every situation, whether he comes within the death-iddah or after the iddah passes, and whether after the second marriage and seclusion and intercourse or before. As clarified by Hadrat Shams al-A'imma in al-Mabsut. One necessary point worthy of attention is that in the well-known Maliki school, there is also a condition for the loss of the first husband's right after intimacy with the second husband, which is not mentioned in the books of the scholars of Medina; it is present in a reliable and authoritative book. That condition is that the second husband should not have knowledge that this woman's husband is missing. And if he knows that her husband is missing, then even after the second husband's entry and intimacy upon return, the first husband's marriage will remain and she will go to him. Similarly, as in Mukhtasar al-Tahlil and its commentary by Allamah al-Dardir, page 200... [continued with Arabic quote]
Source pages p. 119

Commentary
This passage presents a detailed legal ruling regarding a missing person (mafqud) who returns after the qadi has declared him dead. The text is a discussion of a specific problematic case requiring a legal stratagem (hila). The author begins by identifying two scenarios: first, the missing husband returns before the second husband has had valid seclusion (khalwat sahiha) with the wife; second, he returns after the second husband has had valid seclusion. The first scenario is unanimous: the wife remains with the first husband. The second scenario is where the schools differ. The Maliki well-known position is that the wife stays with the second husband. The Hanafi position, as stated by Imam Abu Hanifa and clarified by Shams al-A'imma in al-Mabsut, is that the wife always goes to the first husband regardless of timing or consummation. The passage then adds a nuance to the Maliki school: a condition that the second husband must be unaware that the woman's husband was missing; if he knew, then even after consummation, the first husband's marriage revives. The text quotes from Maliki sources to support this condition. Overall, this ruling illustrates the Hanafi approach to protecting the original marriage and the importance of intent and knowledge in determining legal outcomes.

Key Terms
خلوت صحیحہ (khalwat sahiha): Valid seclusion where a man and woman are alone in a place where intercourse is not physically or legally prevented, and there is no impediment to intimacy.
مفقود (mafqud): A person who has disappeared and whose whereabouts and status (alive or dead) are unknown.
عدت وفات (iddat al-wafat): The waiting period observed by a widow after her husband's death, which is four months and ten days.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Abu Hanifa, what happens to the wife if the missing husband returns after the qadi has declared him dead and the wife has already remarried and consummated the marriage?
A) The wife stays with the second husband.
B) The wife returns to the first husband.
C) The wife may choose between the two husbands.
D) The marriage is dissolved and the wife must observe a new 'iddah for both.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school always preserves the first marriage; the Maliki school has a condition based on the second husband's knowledge of the missing status.

The Return of the Missing Husband: Hanafi Ruling and Maliki Condition
Hanafi ruling with inter-madhhab comparison and fatwa hierarchy note

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the unconditional Hanafi ruling on the return of a missing husband, explain the Maliki condition, and apply the fatwa hierarchy to determine when a mufti may adopt another madhhab.

Core Idea
In Hanafi law, a missing husband's return invalidates his wife's remarriage unconditionally; Maliki conditions it on the second husband's ignorance.

This passage from al-Hilat al-Najijah presents the established Hanafi rule regarding a missing husband (mafqud) who returns after being declared dead and after his wife has remarried. The author contrasts it with a Maliki condition and emphasizes that for a Hanafi mufti, there is no need to resort to another madhhab in this scenario. We will parse the legal rule, the inter-madhhab difference, and the fatwa hierarchy implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core Hanafi ruling
State exactly what happens to the marriage when the missing husband returns, according to Imam Abu Hanifa.
The ruling applies in all circumstances: whether the second marriage was consummated or not, and regardless of timing.
2
Analyze the unconditional nature
Note that the Hanafi position does not condition the first husband's right on the second husband's awareness.
This is the key difference from the Maliki school.
3
Extract the Maliki condition
From the text, identify the condition that the Maliki school imposes for the first husband's right to be lost.
The second husband must be unaware of the missing status.
4
Evaluate the mufti's choice
Understand the author's conclusion that a Hanafi mufti should not follow the Maliki view in this case, as no necessity exists.
This reflects the fatwa hierarchy principle: resort to other madhhabs only in severe hardship.

But if the missing person returns after the ruling of his death? In the treatise, the madhhab of Imam al-A'zam Imam Abu Hanifa is that if the missing person returns even after being declared dead, his wife will be given to him in every case, whether he comes within the waiting period of death or after the waiting period has passed, and whether the second marriage occurred and after seclusion and intercourse or before. As Shams al-A'imma clarified in al-Mabsut and said: [the translation of the Arabic passage is corrupted]. A necessary point worthy of attention is that in the Maliki school as well, after the second husband's intimacy, the first husband's right is lost only under one condition, which is not mentioned in the writings of the scholars of Medina, and it is not known why its mention was omitted from the reliable and authoritative books of our region. That condition is that the second husband should not be aware that this woman's husband is missing. And if he is aware that her husband is missing, then even after the second husband's entry and intimacy, the first husband's marriage remains valid and she will be given to him. Then follows a corrupted Arabic text from al-Dardir. And then: In Mizan al-Sha'rani, it is narrated: 'Among that is the saying of the Hanafis: when a missing person returns after his wife married after the waiting period, the second marriage is invalidated and she is for the first husband, even if the second has had intercourse with her; he must pay the mahr al-mithl, and she is separated from the second and returns to the first.' Translation and summary of the above statement: In Mizan al-Sha'rani, volume 3, it is that Imam Abu Hanifa said: When a missing person returns while his wife has contracted a second marriage, that second marriage becomes void and the woman belongs to the first husband. And if the second husband has had intercourse with the woman, he must pay the mahr al-mithl, and the woman is separated from the other person and observes the waiting period, then she returns to the first husband. For a Hanafi to adopt a non-Hanafi madhhab: It is permissible for a Hanafi to give a fatwa according to a non-Hanafi madhhab in severe necessity, such as cases of the missing wife's waiting period etc. But in the case of the missing person's return, there is no need to act upon another madhhab. Therefore, in this second situation, i.e., when the missing person returns, [the Hanafi ruling suffices].
Source pages p. 120

Commentary
This passage from al-Hilat al-Najijah addresses the Hanafi ruling on a missing husband (mafqud) who returns after being declared dead and after his wife has remarried. The passage type is a legal ruling with inter-madhhab comparison. The author begins by stating the Hanafi position: according to Imam Abu Hanifa, the return of the missing husband invalidates any subsequent marriage of his wife, regardless of whether the second husband has consummated the marriage or not. The wife goes back to the first husband in all cases. This is the relied-upon Hanafi position (Zahir al-Riwayah). The author then notes a Maliki condition for the loss of the first husband's right: that the second husband must not have known that the woman had a missing husband. If he knew, the first marriage remains intact even after the second husband's intimacy. The author quotes a garbled passage from a Maliki source (al-Dardir) and then notes that even according to the well-known Maliki position, the difference only applies when the second husband is unaware. The author then cites Mizan al-Sha'rani confirming the Hanafi view. Finally, the author makes a methodological point: a Hanafi mufti may, in cases of dire necessity, issue a fatwa according to a non-Hanafi madhhab (e.g., for the wife's waiting period matters). However, in the case of the missing husband's return, the Hanafi ruling already provides a clear and complete solution, so there is no need to resort to another madhhab. This underscores the principle that while following another madhhab is permissible in hardship, it is not required when the Hanafi school offers a valid ruling.

Key Terms
مفقود (mafqud): A missing person, specifically a husband whose whereabouts are unknown and who has been declared dead by a judge after a waiting period
عدت (iddah): The waiting period a woman must observe after the death of her husband or divorce
مهر مثل (mahr al-mithl): The customary dowry appropriate for a woman of her status

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi madhhab, what happens if a missing husband returns after his wife has remarried and the second husband has consummated the marriage?
A) The second marriage remains valid and the wife stays with the second husband.
B) The second marriage is invalidated and the wife returns to the first husband, but the second husband keeps the mahr.
C) The second marriage is invalidated and the wife returns to the first husband, and the second husband must pay mahr al-mithl.
D) The wife chooses which husband to stay with.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi ruling on the return of a missing husband is unconditional, and a mufti should not leave the madhhab without necessity.

Missing Husband's Wife: Waiting Period and Legal Stratagem (Hila)
Fatwa on missing husband's wife waiting period, child lineage, and Maliki concession

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the conditions under which a Hanafi judge may adopt the Maliki waiting period (one year) for a missing husband's wife as a legal stratagem to prevent harm, and identify the correct ruling for the child's lineage.

Core Idea
When fear of sin is imminent, a Hanafi judge may adopt the Maliki one-year waiting period as a hila.

This fatwa by Ashraf Ali Thanwi addresses one of the most sensitive cases in family law: the missing husband's wife. The Hanafi default requires a four-year waiting period before dissolving the marriage, but what if the wife cannot remain chaste? The fatwa opens the door to the Maliki ruling of one year as a hila, but with strict conditions. It also settles the child's lineage after remarriage and the obligation of mahr after valid seclusion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core issue and the default Hanafi ruling
State that the woman is a missing husband's wife and the Hanafi position requires a four-year waiting period before dissolution.
The default is based on Umar's practice and precaution.
2
Recognize the Maliki alternative and its basis
Note that the Maliki school allows dissolution after one year when there is fear of zina, based on preventing hardship.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf, not a weaker opinion.
3
Analyze the conditions for applying the hila
List the conditions: the wife must have waited a considerable period, she expresses fear of falling into sin, and the judge sees strong circumstantial evidence.
The fatwa warns against seeking excuses — the hila is only for genuine necessity.
4
Determine the ruling on mahr and child lineage
After valid khalwa (seclusion), full specified mahr is due. The child born after valid remarriage is attributed to the second husband.
Refer to Al-Shami's Radd al-Muhtar for lineage.
5
Apply the fatwa hierarchy and issue the mufta bihi
The mufta bihi in normal circumstances is the four-year waiting period, but the judge may adopt the Maliki view as a hila when conditions are met.
This ruling is from the later fatwa literature, not from Zahir al-Riwayah.

Source pages p. 123

Commentary
This passage is a fatwa by Ashraf Ali Thanwi addressing the case of a missing husband's wife (zawjah mafqud). It covers two main issues: (1) the waiting period before the marriage can be dissolved, and (2) the lineage (nasab) of any child born after remarriage. The fatwa also discusses a legal stratagem (hila) by permitting the judge to adopt the Maliki school's ruling to reduce the waiting period under necessity. The text begins with a ruling about a woman who has been approached (mawtu'a) and then returns to her first husband, referencing Al-Shami and Al-Kafi. It then discusses the mahr obligation after valid khalwa, referencing various authorities. The core argument is that if the wife cannot remain chaste and there is a strong fear of sin, the judge may reduce the waiting period from the Hanafi norm of four years to one year, following the Maliki opinion. However, the scholars of Saharanpur insist on four years as a precaution. The author concludes that where there is strong evidence of imminent zina, adopting the one-year opinion is permissible, but one should not seek excuses. The child's lineage is established from the second husband, as stated by Al-Shami and the Maliki school.

Key Terms
زوجہ مفقود (zawjah mafqud): A woman whose husband is missing and whose whereabouts or life are unknown.
عدت (iddah): The waiting period a woman must observe after dissolution of marriage or death of husband before remarrying.
تفریق (tafriq): Judicial dissolution of marriage (faskh) ordered by the qadi.
نسب (nasab): Legal lineage or parentage, which establishes the child's relationship to the father.
مہر (mahr): Mandatory bridal gift from husband to wife upon marriage.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the fatwa, what is the minimum waiting period after which a Hanafi judge may dissolve the marriage of a missing husband's wife when adopting the Maliki view as a hila?
A) Four years
B) One year
C) Six months
D) Immediately upon application

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi default waiting period for a missing husband's wife is four years, but under necessity and with strong evidence of imminent sin, a judge may adopt the Maliki one-year ruling as a legal stratagem (hila) to prevent greater harm.

The Procedure for Sending Notice to an Absent Husband
Details the method of sending judicial notice to an absent husband via two witnesses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi procedure for sending judicial notice to an absent husband and identify the exception when witnesses cannot be sent.

Core Idea
Judicial notice to an absent husband must be delivered by two witnesses who testify; post is insufficient.

This passage addresses a practical question in family law: how does a wife pursue maintenance from an absent husband? The Hanafi school sets a clear procedure requiring personal delivery by two witnesses to ensure certainty of testimony. This lesson unpacks that procedure and the exceptional case when the husband is inaccessible.

Guided Path
1
Identify the prerequisite
The wife must first demand maintenance (mutalaba) from the place of causation (the husband's absence). If she drops the demand after the husband responds, no separation (tafriq) is granted.
This establishes that the wife's persistent demand is a condition for further action.
2
Outline the normal procedure
The judge must prepare a notice and hand it to two reliable witnesses, who personally deliver it to the absent husband, obtain his response, and return to testify before the judge.
The judge decides based on their testimony, not the written notice alone.
3
Recognise the rejection of postal service
Sending the notice via post or any means that does not involve live testimony is explicitly deemed insufficient.
This underscores the importance of direct witness testimony in Hanafi judicial procedure.
4
Note the cross-madhhab support
The passage cites that both the Hanafi and Maliki schools agree on this procedure, as stated in the work al-Qadi ila al-Qadi.
This adds weight to the ruling.
5
Identify the exception for impossibility
If the husband is in an inaccessible location, the judge may proceed without sending witnesses, after thorough investigation, based on necessity (darura).
This exception is derived from a narration of Allamah A'zam Bashi.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Demand from the place of cause is a condition. So if after the answer of that absent person comes, the woman drops the demand, then separation will not be done. Sending notice is necessary. The method: when the judge sends the judgment notice to that absent person, sending via post etc. is not sufficient. Rather its form is that the judgment letter (notice) should be heard by two reliable persons and handed over to them to take to the absent person. These two persons should deliver the writing to the absent person and ask him for a response, i.e., take his response to the claim, so that when they return they can testify about it. And if the person gives some answer, they should testify to it. Summary: the judge should decide on the basis of the testimony of these two, not consider mere letter sufficient. And this procedure is clearly proven from the Hanafi school and the Maliki school, as is clear from the book al-Qadi ila al-Qadi. And Allamah Dardir said that it is necessary for those two witnesses to testify that this is the writing of such-and-such judge (or Shar'i court) and that the judge made them witnesses on that writing which they bring with them. If the absent husband is in a distant country; if absent, in a non-accessible place etc., such that despite full effort and possible endeavor, sending someone is impossible, then in the aforementioned exigency there is also scope that without sending anyone, the ruler (Shar'i) or his deputy etc., the Shar'i ruler investigates the incident and after due procedure as above, orders separation, as is in the tenth [narration?] of Hadrat Allamah A'zam Bashi.
Source pages p. 132

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on the procedure for sending judicial notice to an absent husband in a maintenance claim case. The author states that the wife's demand (for maintenance) from the place of causation (i.e., the husband's absence) is a condition for initiating the process. If the husband responds and the wife drops her demand, separation (tafriq) should not be granted. The key rule: sending the notice via post or any means that does not involve live testimony is insufficient. Instead, the judge must hand over the notice to two reliable witnesses who take it personally to the absent husband, convey the claim, bring back his response, and testify before the judge. The judge then decides based on their testimony. This procedure is affirmed by both the Hanafi and Maliki schools. The author quotes Allamah Dardir (Maliki jurist) who adds that the witnesses must specifically testify that the document is from the judge and that the judge made them witnesses on it. In extreme cases where the husband is in an inaccessible location and sending witnesses is impossible, the judge may proceed without sending anyone, after thorough investigation, as per a narration from Allamah A'zam Bashi. This shows the Hanafi principle of necessity (darura) relaxing normal procedural requirements.

Key Terms
مطالبہ (mutalaba): The wife's demand for maintenance from the absent husband.
تفریق (tafriq): Judicial separation granted to the wife due to husband's failure to provide maintenance.
گواہ (gawah): Two reliable male witnesses appointed by the judge to deliver the notice and testify.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) By registered post or courier
B) Through two reliable witnesses who deliver the notice and testify
C) By publishing in a newspaper
D) Through a single messenger without testimony

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi procedure, judicial notice to an absent husband must be delivered by two witnesses who testify; only extreme impossibility allows an exception.
