Absent Husband's Return After Tafriq: Within or After 'Iddah
Explains scenarios when the husband returns after tafriq ruling, including within 'iddah and after.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two scenarios of an absent husband's return after a tafriq ruling and apply the condition for reviving the marriage.

Core Idea
If the husband returns within 'iddah and pays maintenance, he can revoke; after 'iddah, he needs to prove the wife's claim was false to reclaim her.

This passage from Al-Hilatul Najija deals with the consequences when a husband who was absent and had a tafriq ruling against him for non-maintenance returns. The ruling differentiates between return during 'iddah and after, and also considers whether the wife's claim was false.

Guided Path
1
Identify temporal scenarios
Determine whether the husband returns within the wife's 'iddah (waiting period) or after it has ended. This is the first distinction the author makes.
2
Within 'iddah: right to raj'ah
If the husband returns within 'iddah and is willing to pay maintenance, he has the right to take her back (raj'ah) without a new contract. If he does not, separation becomes final after 'iddah.
Raj'ah is normally for talaq, but here extended to tafriq by analogy.
3
After 'iddah: need to prove false claim
If the husband returns after 'iddah, he can only reclaim his wife if he proves that her claim against him was false—e.g., that he paid maintenance, sent money, or she forgave it.
The burden of proof lies on the husband.
4
Consequence of proof vs no proof
If he proves the claim false, the original marriage is considered intact, and any subsequent marriage of the wife is void. If he cannot prove, the wife does not return to him.
No new marriage or mahr is needed if he proves his case.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فیصلہ کے بعد اگر غائب آجائے اگر نائب شخص طلاق کا فیصلہ ہو جانے کے بعد حاضر ہو جائے تو اس کی دو صورتیں ہیں ایک یہ کہ عدت کے اندر اندر واپس آجائے اور باقاعدہ خرچہ (نان و نفقہ) وغیرہ دینے پر آمادہ ہو اس صورت میں تو اُس کو رجعت کا حتی ہے اگر رجعت کرے گا تو رجعت صحیح ہو جائے گی اور اگر رجعت نہ کرے تو عدت کے بعد اس کے نکاح سے بالکل علیحدہ ہو جائے گی۔ اگر عورت کا دعوی غلط ثابت ہو جائے یہ کہ مدت ختم ہو چکنے کے بعد دوالیس آیا ہو سوا س میں پر تفصیل ہے کہ اگر اُس نے عورت کے دعوے کے خلاف کوئی بات ثابت کر دی مثلاً یہ کہ میں نے اُس کو پیشگی شرح دے دیا تھا ۔ یا یہ کہ وہاں سے بھینچا رہتا تھا یہ کہ عورت نے ہفتہ معاف کر دیا تھا تب تو اس کو ہر حال میں بیوی مل جاوے گی بینی خواہ وہ عورت عدت کے بعد نکاح ثانی بھی کہو چکی ہو تی کہ اگر شوہر نانی سے اولاد بھی ہو چکی ہو تب بھی شوہر اول ہی کا نکاح باقی سمجھا جائے گا اور شوہرثانی کا نکاح اب باطل قرار دیا جائے گا۔ اور اگر خاوند نے عورت کے دعوے کے خلاف کوئی بات ثابت نہ کی تو عورت اُس کو نہ ملے گی ۔ کیونکہ عدت ختم ہونے کے بعد رجعت کا حق نہیں رہتا ۔ وهذا كله مصرح في الرواية الرابعة عشر د السادسته عشر . اور دوسری صورت کی پہلی شق میں جو شو ہر اول کو عورت ملے گی اُس کو نہ تجدید نکاح کی ضرورت ہے نہ تجدید نہر کی ۔ البتہ شوہر ثانی عد المرأة اذا اسقطت النفقة عن زوجها يلزمها الاسقاط عند المالكية وليس لها ان ترجيح كما في الروايات الاولى
After the ruling, if the absent person comes—if the absent person appears after the divorce ruling, there are two scenarios: One, that he returns within the 'iddah and is willing to give regular expenses (bread and maintenance) etc. In that case, he has the right of raj'ah; if he does raj'ah, it will be valid, and if he does not do raj'ah, then after the 'iddah she will be completely separated from his marriage. If the woman's claim is proven false, i.e., after the period ends, he came? ... details: if he proves something contrary to the woman's claim, for example, that he had paid her maintenance in advance, or that he used to send [money] from there, or that the woman had forgiven the maintenance, then he will get the wife in any case, even if the woman has remarried after the 'iddah, even if the second husband has had children from her, even then the first husband's marriage will be considered ongoing and the second husband's marriage will be declared invalid. And if the husband does not prove anything contrary to the woman's claim, then he will not get the woman, because after the 'iddah ends, the right of raj'ah does not remain. And all this is clearly stated in the fourteenth to sixteenth narration. And in the first branch of the second scenario, when the wife is given to the first husband, neither renewal of marriage nor renewal of mahr is needed. However, the second husband... The woman, when she drops maintenance from her husband, the dropping is binding upon her according to the Malikis, and she does not have the right to return [the claim] as in the first narrations.
Source pages p. 133

Commentary
This passage outlines the consequences when an absent husband returns after a tafriq (separation) ruling has been issued due to his failure to provide maintenance. Two main scenarios are described: First, if the husband returns within the wife's 'iddah (waiting period) and is willing to pay maintenance, he has the right to take her back through raj'ah (revocation of the divorce-like separation). If he does not exercise this right, the separation becomes final after 'iddah. Second, if he returns after the 'iddah has ended, the outcome depends on whether the wife's claim was false. If the husband can prove that he had paid maintenance, sent money, or that the wife had forgiven the maintenance, then the original marriage is considered intact despite the tafriq ruling—even if the wife has remarried and had children, the second marriage is void and the wife returns to the first husband without need for new marriage or mahr. If he cannot prove this, the wife does not return to him. The passage ends with a note from the Maliki school: according to them, if a wife drops her claim to maintenance, it is binding and she cannot revert it—unlike the Hanafi position implied.

Key Terms
رجعت (raj'ah): The husband's right to take back his wife during 'iddah after a revocable separation.
عدت ('iddah): The waiting period after separation during which the husband can revoke.
تفریق (tafriq): Judicial separation granted due to the husband's failure to provide maintenance.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what happens if an absent husband returns after the 'iddah has ended and proves that the wife had forgiven his maintenance?
A) The tafriq is upheld and the wife is free to remarry
B) The husband must remarry her with a new contract and mahr
C) The original marriage is considered intact, and any subsequent marriage of the wife is void
D) The wife can choose to stay with either husband

Key Takeaway
An absent husband can reclaim his wife after tafriq if he returns within 'iddah with payment, or after 'iddah by proving the wife's claim false.

Maliki View on Dropping Maintenance: Fatwa Implications
Notes the Maliki ruling on a wife dropping maintenance as a point of ikhtilaf.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Maliki position on a wife dropping maintenance and evaluate its effect on fatwa within the Hanafi tradition.

Core Idea
Malikis hold that a wife's dropping of maintenance is binding; Hanafis differ, affecting tafriq outcomes.

At the end of the previous passage, the author briefly notes a Maliki ruling: if a wife drops her maintenance claim, it is binding and she cannot later revoke it. This lesson isolates that point and explores its significance for a Hanafi mufti who may encounter cross-madhhab situations or talfiq (patching).

Guided Path
1
Identify the Maliki view
Read the final line of the text: the woman, when she drops maintenance, the dropping is binding upon her according to the Malikis, and she does not have the right to return the claim.
This is a rule of isqat (waiver) – a voluntary relinquishment of a right is irrevocable.
2
Compare with the implied Hanafi view
In the main Hanafi discussion, the wife's dropping of maintenance is listed as one example of how the husband can prove the wife's claim false. This suggests that in Hanafi, the dropping may not be permanently binding – the wife could still later claim non-maintenance if the husband stops paying.
The passage does not explicitly state the Hanafi position, but it can be inferred from the context.
3
Consider practical implications for fatwa
If a wife drops maintenance in a Hanafi context and later seeks tafriq for non-maintenance, the mufti must decide whether her dropping bars her claim. According to Maliki, it does; according to Hanafi (implied), it may not. The mufti should be aware of this difference when advising or issuing a fatwa.
In some cases, a Hanafi mufti may adopt the Maliki view as a secondary opinion (talfiq) if it serves the maslahah.

After the ruling, if the absent person comes—if the absent person appears after the divorce ruling, there are two scenarios: One, that he returns within the 'iddah and is willing to give regular expenses (bread and maintenance) etc. In that case, he has the right of raj'ah; if he does raj'ah, it will be valid, and if he does not do raj'ah, then after the 'iddah she will be completely separated from his marriage. And if the husband does not prove anything contrary to the woman's claim, then he will not get the woman, because after the 'iddah ends, the right of raj'ah does not remain. Source pages p. 133

Commentary
This passage outlines the consequences when an absent husband returns after a tafriq (separation) ruling has been issued due to his failure to provide maintenance. Two main scenarios are described: First, if the husband returns within the wife's 'iddah (waiting period) and is willing to pay maintenance, he has the right to take her back through raj'ah (revocation of the divorce-like separation). If he does not exercise this right, the separation becomes final after 'iddah. Second, if he returns after the 'iddah has ended, the outcome depends on whether the wife's claim was false. If the husband can prove that he had paid maintenance, sent money, or that the wife had forgiven the maintenance, then the original marriage is considered intact despite the tafriq ruling—even if the wife has remarried and had children, the second marriage is void and the wife returns to the first husband without need for new marriage or mahr. If he cannot prove this, the wife does not return to him. The passage ends with a note from the Maliki school: according to them, if a wife drops her claim to maintenance, it is binding and she cannot revert it—unlike the Hanafi position implied.

Key Terms
اسقاط (isqat): Waiver or dropping of a right (here, maintenance)
مالکیہ (malikiyyah): The Maliki school of Islamic law

Key Takeaway
The Maliki school holds that a wife's waiver of maintenance is final; Hanafi muftis should know this for cross-madhhab fatwas.

Maliki Ruling on Wife Dropping Maintenance
States the Maliki view that a wife's dropping of maintenance is binding and irrevocable.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Maliki ruling on a wife's dropping of maintenance and identify the contrasting Hanafi position.

Core Idea
In Maliki, a wife's waiver of maintenance is binding and irrevocable; Hanafi allows revocation under conditions.

This passage from Al-Hilatun Najijah presents a Maliki legal ruling that is critical for muftis dealing with trans-madhhab fatwa. Understanding the binding nature of isqat in the Maliki school prevents incorrect application when the questioner follows that school. The Hanafi position, implied by contrast, offers more flexibility.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the school
The act is the wife dropping her claim to maintenance (isqat al-nafaqah). The passage states the ruling according to the Maliki school.
The Maliki school is known for binding isqat; other schools may differ.
2
State the Maliki ruling
The ruling is that the dropping (isqat) is binding upon the wife, and she cannot return it (i.e., cannot later reclaim the maintenance).
The phrase 'يلزمها الاسقاط' indicates legal obligation to abide by the waiver.
3
Note the basis
The passage cites 'as in the first narrations' – referring to foundational Maliki texts.
The exact source of these narrations is not specified, but they are authoritative within the Maliki school.
4
Contrast with the Hanafi position
In Hanafi fiqh, a wife's waiver of maintenance is generally not binding for future rights; she can revoke it under certain conditions (e.g., if not done with full intent or if circumstances change).
This contrast is essential for muftis dealing with parties from different schools.
5
Apply in a fatwa context
When issuing a fatwa, the mufti must follow the school of the questioner. If the wife is Maliki, the fatwa is that she cannot reclaim; if Hanafi, she may be able to.
Ignoring madhhab differences can lead to incorrect and unjust rulings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عد المرأة اذا اسقطت النفقة عن زوجها يلزمها الاسقاط عند المالكية وليس لها ان ترجيح كما في الروايات الاولى
The woman: when she drops maintenance from her husband, the dropping is binding upon her according to the Malikis, and she does not have the right to return it, as in the first narrations.
Source pages p. 133

Commentary
This brief note presents a Maliki legal position: if a wife voluntarily drops her claim to maintenance (for example, in a dispute), that waiver is binding and irrevocable. She cannot later change her mind and demand the maintenance. The Hanafi position, implied to be different, allows the wife to revert to her claim under certain conditions. This distinction is important for a mufti who may need to give fatwa in jurisdictions where Maliki law applies or to parties following the Maliki school.

Key Terms
اسقاط (isqat): Dropping or waiving a legal right, here the right to maintenance.
نفقہ (nafaqah): Maintenance, the financial support a husband must provide to his wife.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
In the Maliki school, a wife's waiver of maintenance is final and irrevocable, a ruling that must be respected when issuing fatwa for Maliki questioners.

Ruling on Return of Absent Husband After Judicial Separation
Ruling on return of absent husband after decree

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two scenarios when an absent husband returns after a judicial separation decree, and apply the conditions for each scenario to determine the marital status.

Core Idea
A husband's return within iddah revives marriage via ruju'; return after iddah requires proof of wife's false claim to revive marriage, even if she remarried.

This passage from Ashraf Ali Thanwi's Hilatun Najiyah addresses a critical practical scenario: an absent husband returns after a qadi has issued a divorce decree due to his absence. The ruling distinguishes between return during the iddah and after, and hinges on the husband's ability to prove the wife's claim false. Understanding this rule is essential for muftis dealing with contemporary family law cases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two main scenarios
Distinguish between the husband returning during the iddah period versus after the iddah period has ended. This determines the applicable rules.
2
Analyze return within iddah
If he returns within iddah and is willing to provide maintenance, he has the right of ruju'. If he exercises ruju', the marriage is revived; otherwise, after iddah the separation becomes final.
3
Analyze return after iddah
If he returns after iddah, the outcome depends on whether he can prove the wife's claim false (e.g., advance payment of maintenance, detention, or waiver).
4
Determine effect of proof
If he proves falsehood, the first marriage is revived even if the wife remarried and had children; the second marriage is void. If he cannot prove, the wife is not returned.
5
Note ancillary rulings
When the wife is returned, no new marriage contract or dower is needed. However, if she had valid seclusion with the second husband, she must observe iddah before relations with the first husband. The second husband's dower depends on whether valid seclusion occurred.

Source Text

After the decision, if the absent one comes: if the deputy for the divorce decision appears after the decision, then there are two scenarios: One: that he returns within the iddah and is ready to give regular maintenance (nafaqah) etc. In that case, he has the right of ruju'; if he performs ruju', the ruju' will be valid, and if he does not perform ruju', then after the iddah he will be completely separated from her marriage. If the woman's claim proves false, i.e., he came after the period ended, then there is detail: if he proves something contrary to the woman's claim, for example, that he had given her advance maintenance, or that he was detained there, or that the woman had waived the maintenance, then the wife will be given to him in any case, even if that woman has performed a second marriage after the iddah, even if she has had children from the second husband, then the marriage of the first husband will be considered remaining and the marriage of the second husband will be declared void. And if the husband does not prove anything contrary to the woman's claim, then the woman will not be given to him, because after the iddah ends, the right of ruju' does not remain. All this is explicitly stated in the fourteenth and sixteenth riwayahs. And in the first branch of the second scenario, for the first husband who gets the woman, neither renewal of the marriage contract nor renewal of the dower is required. However, if a valid seclusion (khalwat sahihah) has occurred with the second husband, then iddah is obligatory. And regarding the obligation of dower upon the second husband, the same detail applies as passed in the discussion of the missing person, i.e., if valid seclusion has occurred with him, then the full (dower) is obligatory, otherwise it is completely dropped. Similarly in other annulments. Also in the rulings of the missing person it has passed that the iddah will be spent in the house of the first husband. So consider.
Source pages p. 133

Commentary
This passage is a ruling about what happens when an absent husband returns after a judicial separation (faysalah) has been issued due to his absence. It presents two main scenarios: (1) If the husband returns during the iddah period and is willing to provide maintenance, he has the right to take his wife back (ruju'). If he does not take her back, the separation becomes final after iddah. (2) If the husband returns after the iddah, the outcome depends on whether he can prove that the wife's claim (which led to the separation) was false. If he proves, e.g., that he had already paid maintenance, was unlawfully detained, or the wife had waived maintenance, then the marriage with the first husband is revived, even if the wife has remarried and had children; the second marriage is nullified. If he cannot prove falsehood, the wife is not returned to him. The passage also clarifies that if the wife is returned to the first husband, no renewal of the marriage contract or dower is needed. However, if she had valid seclusion with the second husband, she must observe iddah before the first husband can have relations with her. The dower for the second husband follows the rules for missing persons: full dower if valid seclusion occurred, otherwise nothing. This ruling is based on specific riwayahs from the Hanafi school (fourteenth, sixteenth, and twenty-ninth to thirty-fourth).

Key Terms
رجعت (ruju'): The husband's right to take back his wife during the iddah period after a revocable divorce, without a new marriage contract.
عدت (iddah): The waiting period a woman must observe after divorce or death of husband, during which she cannot remarry.
خلوت صحیحہ (khalwat sahihah): Valid seclusion between a husband and wife that is not prevented by any legal impediment, sufficient to establish the same rights as actual intercourse regarding dower and iddah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The absent husband's return within iddah revives marriage via ruju'; return after iddah requires proof of the wife's false claim to revive the marriage, potentially voiding any subsequent marriage.

Establishing Hurmat Musahara through Lustful Acts
Explaining conditions for marriage affinity prohibition through illicit acts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the conditions for Hurmat Musahara to determine when a marriage becomes permanently invalid.

Core Idea
Lustful touch, kiss, or look with lust can permanently forbid marriage relatives—even if unintentional.

Understanding Hurmat Musahara is essential for a mufti because a single prohibited act can annul an existing marriage. This lesson unpacks the Hanafi conditions under which such affinity prohibitions arise from zina, lustful touch, kiss, or look. The text clarifies the role of lust, the barrier, and ejaculation, and emphasizes that intention is irrelevant. Through a careful parsing exercise, you will learn to identify the precise scenarios that trigger this permanent prohibition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Acts
List the four types of acts that can cause Hurmat Musahara: zina, lustful touch (lams), lustful kiss (taqbil), and lustful look at the private parts (nazar).
Each act must be committed with lust.
2
Determine Lust Requirement
For lams and taqbil, it is sufficient that at least one party (the man or woman) has lust. For nazar, only the looker must have lust.
Lust in the other party alone does not suffice for nazar.
3
Check for Barriers and Ejaculation
If there is a thick cloth barrier that prevents feeling body heat, lams/taqbil do not cause Hurmat Musahara. Also, if ejaculation occurs from any of these acts, the prohibition is not established.
Ejaculation (inzal) acts as a preventer (mani).
4
Understand Consequences
Once Hurmat Musahara is established, the man's ascendants and descendants (including foster relatives) become permanently forbidden to the woman, and vice versa. The existing marriage is annulled and requires talaq.
The couple must separate immediately.
5
Apply the Rule
Use a hypothetical scenario, such as a man unintentionally touching his mother-in-law's hand with lust, no barrier, no ejaculation. Conclude that his wife becomes permanently forbidden.
Intention is irrelevant; even an unintentional act triggers the prohibition if all conditions are met.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حرمت مصاہرہ اگر کوئی شخص کسی عورت سے زنا کرے یا شہوت کے ساتھ اس کو صرف ہاتھ لگائے یا شہوت سے بوسہ لے یا شرم گاہ کے اندرونی حصہ کو شہوت سے دیکھ لے تو ان سب صورتوں میں حرمت مصاہرہ قائم ہو جاتی ہے۔ یعنی اس مرد پر اس عورت کی بیٹی اور ماں وغیرہ سب اصول و فروع نسبی و رضاعی حرام ہو جاتے ہیں اور اس عورت پر اس مرد کا بیٹا اور باپ سب اصول و فروع نسبی و رضاعی حرام ہو جاتے ہیں ۔ اسی طرح عورت کسی مرد کو شہوت سے ہاتھ لگا دے یا شہوت سے اُس کا بوسہ لے یا عضو مخصوص پر نظر شہوت ڈالے تب بھی مصاہرت کا علاقہ قائم ہو کر مرد پر عورت کے تمام اصول وفروع نسبی و رضاعی اور عورت پر مرد کے تمام اصول و فروع نسبی و رضاعی ہمیشہ کے لیے حرام ہو جاتے ہیں اور حرمت مصاہرہ کے لیے ان افعال کا قصداً کرنا شرط نہیں۔ بلکہ اگر کسی سے بے خبری میں بھی کوئی فعل سرزد ہو جائے مثلاً بیوی سمجھ کر خوشدامن کو شہوت کی حالت میں ہاتھ لگا دیا تب بھی بیوی حرام ہو جاتی ہے۔ حرمت مصاہرہ کے لیے ان افعال کا قصداً کرنا شرط نہیں۔ بلکہ اگر کسی سے بے خبری میں بھی کوئی فعل سرزد ہو جائے تو بھی وہی حکم ہے۔ عورت کی شہوت کا حکم: لمس و تقبیل یعنی چھونے اور بوسہ لینے کے وقت اگر مرد کو شہوت نہ تھی مگر عورت کو ہو گئی تب بھی یہی حکم ہے۔ اسی طرح اگر عورت نے ہاتھ لگایا ہے یا بوسہ لیا ہے تب بھی دونوں میں سے ایک کو شہوت ہونا کافی ہے۔ البتہ نظر کے موجب حرمت ہونے کے لیے یہ شرط ہے کہ جو دیکھے اُس کو شہوت ہو صرف دوسری طرف سے شہوت ہونا موجب حرمت نہیں۔ اور نیز لمس اور تقبیل میں ایک شرط یہ بھی ہے کہ ایسا کپڑا حائل نہ ہو جو بدن کی گرمی محسوس ہونے کو روک دے پس اگر کسی نے باوجود ایسے کپڑا حائل ہونے کے کپڑے کے اوپر سے مس کیا یا بوسہ لیا ہے تو وہ حرمت مصاہرت کا موجب نہیں۔ نیز ایک شرط یہ بھی ہے کہ ان افعال کی وجہ سے انزال نہ ہو گیا ہو کیونکہ اگر کسی لمس و تقبیل و نظر ہی سے انزال ہو جائے تو حرمت مصاہرت ثابت نہ ہوگی۔ حرمت مصاہرت واقع ہونے کے بعد بھی طلاق ضروری ہے اگر حرمت مصاہرت کی کوئی صورت پیش آگئی ہو تو عورت کے لیے ضروری ہے کہ ہرگز ہرگز شوہر کے پاس نہ رہے اور نہ شوہر کو قریب آنے دے اور شوہر کو بھی احتیاط لازم ہے کہ ایسی حرکات کا قصداً ارتکاب نہ کرے اور نہ ایسا کوئی کام کرے جس میں کوئی احتمال ہو۔ مثلاً جس کمرہ میں بیوی لیٹی ہے اگر وہاں دوسری مستورات بھی ہوں تو جب تک اُس کو جگا کر اور بات چیت کر کے پورا یقین نہ ہو جائے کہ یہ بیوی ہے اُس وقت تک ہاتھ ہرگز نہ لگے۔ پلنگ میں ہونے وغیرہ کو ہرگز کافی سمجھے کہ اس میں بعض مرتبہ غلطی ہو جاتی ہے۔
Prohibition of marriage affinity (Hurmat Musahara): If a person commits zina with a woman, or touches her only with lust, or kisses her with lust, or looks at the inner part of the private parts with lust, then in all these cases Hurmat Musahara is established. That is, for that man, the woman's daughter and mother etc., all ascendants and descendants by lineage and fosterage become prohibited. And for that woman, the man's son and father etc., all ascendants and descendants by lineage and fosterage become prohibited. Similarly, if a woman touches a man with lust, or kisses him with lust, or looks at his private part with lust, then the relationship of Musahara is established, and for the man, all the woman's ascendants and descendants by lineage and fosterage, and for the woman, all the man's ascendants and descendants by lineage and fosterage become permanently prohibited. For Hurmat Musahara, it is not a condition to perform these acts intentionally. Rather, even if one of these acts occurs unintentionally, for example, thinking her to be his wife, he touches his mother-in-law with lust, then even his wife becomes prohibited. The ruling regarding the woman's lust: At the time of touch (lams) and kiss (taqbil), if the man had no lust but the woman did, the same ruling applies. Similarly, if the woman touched or kissed, it is sufficient that one of the two had lust. However, for prohibition through looking, it is a condition that the one who looks has lust; the other having lust alone is not sufficient. Also, in touch and kiss, there is a condition that there is no cloth barrier preventing the sensation of body heat. So if someone touches or kisses over a cloth that is a barrier, then it does not cause Hurmat Musahara. Another condition is that no ejaculation (inzal) occurs because of these acts. If ejaculation occurs from touch, kiss, or look then Hurmat Musahara is not established. After Hurmat Musahara occurs, divorce (talaq) is necessary. If any situation of Hurmat Musahara occurs, it is necessary for the woman to never stay with her husband, nor let the husband come near, and the husband must also be careful not to intentionally commit such acts, nor to do any act in which there is any possibility. For example, in the room where the wife is lying, if there are other women as well, then until he wakes her up and speaks to her to be completely sure that this is his wife, he should not touch her at all. He should never consider being in the bed etc. sufficient because sometimes mistakes occur.
Source pages p. 142

Commentary
This passage is a complete ruling on the establishment of Hurmat Musahara (prohibition of marriage affinity) through illicit acts other than actual marriage. The ruling is from the Hanafi school and is essential for muftis to know because it can annul a marriage unexpectedly. The passage first lists the acts that cause Hurmat Musahara: actual zina, lustful touching, kissing, or looking at the private parts with lust. Note that it must be with lust; casual unintentional contact without desire does not establish this prohibition. The effect is that the relatives of the parties become permanently forbidden to each other, including foster relatives. Importantly, intention is not required; even if the act happens by mistake (e.g., mistaking mother-in-law for wife), the prohibition is established. For touch and kiss, it is sufficient that one party had lust; for looking, the looker must have lust. Two conditions prevent the prohibition: the presence of a thick cloth barrier that prevents feeling body heat, and ejaculation (inzal) resulting from the act. If ejaculation occurs, the act is considered more like a minor impurity and does not create affinity. Finally, once Hurmat Musahara is established, the couple must separate immediately, and divorce is necessary. The husband must take extreme caution to avoid such situations, especially when other women are present.

Key Terms
حرمت مصاہرہ (Ḥurmat Muṣāhara): Permanent prohibition of marriage between a man and a woman's relatives (and vice versa) established through illicit acts like zina, lustful touch, kiss, or look.
لمس (Lams): Touching with lust, whether with hand or other body part, without a thick cloth barrier.
تقبیل (Taqbīl): Kissing with lust, also without barrier.
شہوت (Shahwat): Sexual desire or lust. The passage requires that at least one party (for lams/taqbil) or the looker (for nazar) experience this.
انزال (Inzāl): Ejaculation. Its occurrence from lams, taqbil, or nazar prevents establishment of Hurmat Musahara.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following conditions prevents Hurmat Musahara from being established after a lustful touch?
A) The touch was unintentional.
B) There was a thick cloth barrier that blocked the sensation of body heat.
C) The person touched had no lust.
D) The touch occurred at night.

Key Takeaway
Hurmat Musahara arises from specific lustful acts (zina, lams, taqbil, nazar) with specific conditions, and intention is irrelevant—making it a critical ruling for muftis.

Rulings on Hurmat Musaharat After Marriage
Explains consequences and required actions when hurmat musaharat occurs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on emergent hurmat musaharat and apply the requirement that separation must be by husband's pronouncement or qadi's decree.

Core Idea
Emergent hurmat musaharat requires husband or qadi to separate; wife cannot remarry without it.

In this passage, Ashraf Ali Thanwi explains the consequences of actions that create prohibited affinity after marriage. The key issue is how separation is effected, with a nuanced dispute among Hanafi authorities. Understanding this distinction is crucial for issuing correct fatwas in family law cases.

Guided Path
1
Define hurmat musaharat and identify the acts that cause it
What actions trigger the prohibition? (touch with desire, intentional or mistaken)
The act must involve a mahram by affinity (e.g., mother-in-law, father-in-law).
2
State the immediate required actions
What must the husband do? (pronounce separation/divorce) What must the wife do? (leave immediately)
The wife cannot stay with the husband even if he refuses.
3
Distinguish original vs. emergent prohibition
Why does the ruling on who can initiate separation differ? Original: both spouses annul. Emergent: only husband or qadi.
This distinction resolves the apparent conflict between al-Bahr and al-Nahr.
4
Identify the disputed point
Who can effect separation in emergent cases? The wife according to some authorities, or only husband/qadi according to the relied-upon view.
The passage sides with husband/qadi.
5
Apply the tarjih
Which position is mufta bihi and why? The position requiring husband's pronouncement or qadi's ruling is preferred due to precaution and authority of later scholars.
Intent (qasd) is known only by husband's statement, so woman cannot unilaterally act.

If an act occurs, for example, thinking it is his wife, he touches his mother-in-law in a state of desire, then also the wife becomes forbidden. Therefore, it is necessary for the husband to be extremely careful with his wife's ascendants and descendants (female), and for the woman with the man's aforementioned ascendants and descendants, because besides severe sin, there is a great corruption that a relationship of prohibited affinity occurs between husband and wife. That is, if from the husband an act occurs with any of his wife's female ascendants or descendants, or someone from the wife's female ascendants or descendants commits an act with a man that causes prohibited affinity, for example, with desire he says to the mother-in-law, 'touch', or the wife does something with her husband's male ascendants or descendants, like father-in-law, that makes prohibited affinity necessary, or the father-in-law commits such an act, then in all these cases the wife becomes forever forbidden to her husband, whether the act was done intentionally or by mistake; in every state this is the ruling, as was just explained. After the occurrence of prohibited affinity, divorce is necessary. If any case of prohibited affinity occurs, it is necessary for the woman to never ever stay with the husband, nor allow the husband to come near, nor [???] and not intentionally perform such acts, nor do any act in which there is any probability. For example, in the room where the wife lies if there are other women, then until he wakes her and converses to be fully certain that it is his wife, he should never touch; being on the bed etc. should never be considered sufficient because sometimes a mistake occurs. It is also necessary for the husband to immediately separate such a woman and verbally indicate separation, for example by saying 'I have left you' or 'I have divorced you.' After that, when the waiting period passes, it will be permissible for the wife to marry elsewhere. But if the husband adopts evil religion and does not separate her, then it is extremely necessary for the woman to leave that person by any possible means, because remaining in a relationship with him is forbidden. Until the husband does not say 'I have separated this woman' or the judge does not effect a legal separation, the woman cannot validly marry another person. As it is in Durr al-Mukhtar: ... In this context, the words of Shami regarding the proof of prohibited affinity for Shami's text in Kitab al-Nikah, second volume: 'And as for what was mentioned in 'Umdat Radd al-Mukhtar and likewise in al-Bahr, that the separation is from the husband as well as from the wife, it is specific to when the prohibition is original, as when a woman marries a person who establishes prohibited affinity through marriage or fosterage before marriage, then it is obligatory upon each spouse to annul it, and each one is independent in this separation. It is not the same in the case of an emergent prohibition after marriage, and that the separation between them is realized by the husband or the judge's separation. This is the picture of combining the two statements, and thereby the difference between the statements of al-Bahr and al-Nahr mentioned in al-Shamiyya is resolved. And look at the two [???] that cause prohibited affinity, as is not hidden, but if we assume [???]. And apparently intent is not known except by the husband's statement, so there is no benefit to this difference regarding the woman as far as I know, unless a benefit appears in some case. Therefore, it is precautionary to take the statement of those who require separation by pronouncement, especially in corruption.'
Source pages p. 143

Commentary
This passage from Al-Hilatul Najijah addresses the legal consequences of an act that creates hurmat musaharat (prohibited affinity) after marriage. The author begins by reiterating that certain actions, such as touching a mother-in-law with desire while mistaking her for the wife, render the wife permanently forbidden. He emphasizes the need for extreme caution to avoid such situations. Then, he outlines the required steps: immediate separation and verbal pronouncement of divorce by the husband, followed by the wife's waiting period after which she may remarry. If the husband refuses, the woman must leave him, but she cannot remarry until the husband pronounces separation or a qadi (judge) effects a legal separation. The passage then quotes a portion from Shami (Ibn Abidin) which distinguishes between original prohibition (before marriage) and emergent prohibition (after marriage). In the former, both spouses must annul; in the latter, separation must come from the husband or the qadi. The author notes that this resolves an apparent contradiction between al-Bahr and al-Nahr. He concludes that since intent (for the act) is only known by the husband's admission, the precautionary position is to require a verbal separation from the husband, especially in cases of corruption. The authority weight here is from later fatawa, as the author relies on Shami and Durr al-Mukhtar.

Key Terms
حرمت مصاہرت (Hurmat Musaharat): Prohibition due to affinity; when a person marries, certain in-laws become mahram (unmarriageable) permanently.
مفارقة (Mufaraqa): Separation between spouses as a result of the prohibition.
اصل و فروع (Usul wa Furū'): Ascendants and descendants; for a wife, her mother and grandmother are usul (ascendants) and her daughters are furu' (descendants).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position presented in this passage, how must separation be effected in a case of emergent hurmat musaharat after marriage?
A) The wife can separate herself by leaving the house.
B) The husband must pronounce separation or the qadi must rule it.
C) Either spouse can announce the separation.
D) The marriage is automatically annulled without any action.

Key Takeaway
Emergent hurmat musaharat after marriage requires immediate separation effected by the husband's pronouncement or qadi's ruling; the wife cannot unilaterally remarry.

Separation Due to Prohibition of Affinity from Zina: The Ruling and Conditions
Explains the ruling on separation due to prohibition of affinity from zina.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that zina creates hurmah al-musaharah and state the conditions for valid separation (mutaraka).

Core Idea
Zina creates marriage prohibition; verbal separation or qadi's decree is required.

In Hanafi fiqh, committing zina with a relative of one's spouse establishes a permanent prohibition of marriage by affinity (hurmah al-musaharah). This necessitates a formal separation. This lesson unpacks the ruling: why it is wajib to separate, how to effect it verbally, and what happens when the husband refuses.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its consequence
The act is zina (unlawful intercourse). The consequence is hurmah al-musaharah (prohibition of affinity), which makes the current marriage invalid and requires separation.
Zina with a relative of the spouse (e.g., wife's mother or sister) triggers this prohibition.
2
Distinguish original vs. arising prohibition
Original prohibition (asliyyah) exists before marriage (e.g., fosterage). Arising prohibition (tariyyah) occurs after marriage (e.g., zina with a relative). In original prohibition, each spouse is independent in annulment; in arising, only the husband's pronouncement or qadi's decree works.
This distinction resolves apparent contradictions in the sources.
3
Apply the requirement of mutaraka
For arising prohibition, the husband must pronounce separation (e.g., 'I have left you' or 'I have divorced you'). Without this, the wife cannot remarry even after leaving the husband.
A qadi's decree can substitute for the husband's pronouncement.
4
Handle the husband's refusal
If the husband adopts 'bad religion' and refuses to separate, the wife must leave him immediately, but she still needs a pronouncement or qadi's decree to remarry.
Staying with him is haram, but legal separation requires a formal act.

Source Text

It is also necessary upon the husband to immediately separate such a woman and manifest separation verbally, for example by saying 'I have left you' or by saying 'I have divorced you'. And after this saying, after the idda passes, it will be permissible for the wife to marry elsewhere. But if the husband adopts bad religion and does not separate the wife, then by whatever means possible it is essential for the woman to leave that person's company, for having a conjugal relationship with him is forbidden. Until the husband does not say that he has separated this woman, or the qadi does not effect a separation, until then this woman's marriage to another person cannot be valid. As is in Durr al-Mukhtar. In this series, the Shami discusses the proof of prohibition of affinity. The wording of Shami from Kitab al-Nikah, volume 2: 'As for what was mentioned in Radd al-Muhtar and similar in al-Bahr, that the separation is as from the husband so also from the wife, is specific to when the prohibition is original (asli), such as when a woman marries someone who establishes prohibition of affinity and fosterage before marriage. Then it is obligatory upon each of the spouses to annul it, and each of them is independent in this separation. It is not so for the arising prohibition (tariyyah) after marriage; that separation is only by the husband's pronouncement or the qadi's separation. This is the reconciliation between the two statements, and thereby the disagreement between the statements of al-Bahr and al-Nahr mentioned in al-Shamiyya is resolved. And consideration of both causes prohibition of affinity, as is not hidden. However, if they did not intend to address this, and apparently the intention is not known except by the husband's statement, then this difference has no benefit for the woman as far as I know. But if a benefit appears in some situation, then the precaution is to take the statement of the one who required separation by pronouncement, especially in corruption.' The following passage should be noted: 'Prohibition by affinity is established by zina, meaning unlawful intercourse, and the head uncovered? and hair not touching the head with a barrier not eliminating heat? and the original contact? and his looking at her genitals and her looking at his internal passage? and looking through glass? and their branches absolutely.' Al-Shafi'i said under his statement 'also prohibited': He said in al-Bahr: By prohibition of affinity are meant the four prohibitions: prohibition of the woman upon his ascendants and descendants by lineage and fosterage, and prohibition of her ascendants and descendants upon him by lineage and fosterage, due to lawful intercourse. Al-Khars? Vol 2. And he said under his statement 'absolutely': It refers to the ascendants and descendants, even if they are low? And in al-Daray? Sufficient is desire from one of them. Al-Shafi'i said: This is apparent in touching; in looking, desire is considered from the viewer. And also: By prohibition of affinity, the marriage is not removed, so it is not permissible for her to marry another except after separation (mutaraka) and completion of idda. And in Radd al-Muhtar under his statement 'except after separation' meaning? as in al-Bazzaziyya. And the wording of al-Hadi: 'except after the qadi's separation or separation.' And you have known that the marriage is not removed but is annulled. And they explicitly stated in corrupt marriage that separation is only by pronouncement if it is by pronouncement, like 'I have left you' or 'I have released you.' As for the non-consummated one, it is said it is by pronouncement and by leaving with the intention? It is said it is only by pronouncement in both. Al-Shami Vol 2. The ruling in the absence of a Shari'a qadi: If a woman wants to marry elsewhere, she should file a claim with the qadi.
Source pages p. 144

Commentary
This passage is a legal ruling (hukm) on the consequences of zina that creates a prohibition of marriage by affinity (hurmah al-musaharah). The core issue: When a man commits zina with a woman (e.g., his wife's mother or daughter), it prohibits him from marrying her and also his wife? The text explains that the husband must separate from his wife through a clear verbal pronouncement (mutaraka) such as 'I have left you' or 'I have divorced you.' After the wife completes her idda, she may marry another. If the husband refuses, the wife must leave him, as staying is haram. However, without the husband's pronouncement or a qadi's decree, she cannot remarry. The passage then discusses a distinction between original prohibition (asliyyah) — which arises before marriage (e.g., marrying a foster relative) — and arising prohibition (tariyyah) — which arises after marriage (e.g., zina with a relative of the wife). In original prohibition, both spouses are independently required to annul the marriage. In arising prohibition, only the husband's pronouncement or qadi's ruling effects separation. This reconciles apparent contradictions in the sources. The text also quotes al-Shafi'i to note that in the Shafi'i school, touch is considered differently from looking regarding desire. The author concludes that the precaution is to require a verbal pronouncement (using the stronger opinion) especially when corruption is possible. The ruling also covers the case where no qadi is available: the woman must file a claim with a qadi.

Key Terms
المتاركة (al-mutārakah): Legal separation by pronouncement (e.g., 'I have left you') to end a marriage that has become prohibited due to zina or similar.
حرمة المصاهرة (hurmah al-muṣāharah): Prohibition of marriage due to affinity (by marriage or by zina that creates affinity).

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) In both original and arising prohibition
B) In arising prohibition only if the husband refuses
C) In original prohibition, each spouse is independent
D) Never

Key Takeaway
Zina creates prohibition of affinity; for arising prohibition, separation requires the husband's verbal pronouncement or a qadi's decree.

The Binding Nature of Father's Marriage for Minors
Ruling on khiyar al-bulugh for father's marriage

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on khiyar al-bulugh when the father contracts the marriage and identify the exception when an option arises.

Core Idea
Father's marriage for a minor is binding; no annulment upon puberty unless the father was deceived.

Many assume that a minor can always annul a marriage contracted by a guardian upon reaching puberty. However, Hanafi law distinguishes between the father (and grandfather) and other guardians. This lesson clarifies that the father's authority is absolute, making his marriage binding. But there is a narrow exception when the father was tricked about compatibility.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Guardian
Recognize that the father is the foremost guardian for minors (and the grandfather in his absence). This is the default authority for marriage.
The father's role is unique; other guardians do not have the same binding authority.
2
State the Default Ruling
The father's marriage for a minor is binding (lazim). The minor has no option (khiyar al-bulugh) to annul upon reaching puberty.
This holds regardless of whether the marriage is to a compatible (kuf') partner or not, and regardless of mahr adequacy.
3
Note the Scope
The binding nature covers all aspects: it is valid even if the mahr is grossly deficient or the spouse is incompatible.
Gross deficiency in mahr is defined relative to mahr al-mithl.
4
Identify the Exception
If the father was unaware of incompatibility because he relied on false statements from the spouse or in-laws, then a different option (khiyar al-qamat) may arise after puberty.
This exception requires that the father was deceived, not just mistaken.
5
Apply to Analogous Cases
The same binding effect applies when a son marries an insane father (as guardian). The ruling is consistent across these scenarios.
This reinforces the principle that the father's authority is paramount.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
خیار بلوغ۔ ہے نابالغ لڑکے اور لڑکی کا سب سے مقدم ولی باپ ہے۔ اگر باپ نابالغ کا نکاح کردے تو وہ نکاح لازم ہو جاتا ہے یعنی بلوغ کے بعد بھی لڑکے لڑکی کو اس کے فسخ کرانے کا اختیار نہیں رہتا خواہ کفو میں نکاح کیا ہو یا غیر کفو ہیں۔ اور ہر شل مقرر ہوا ہو یا مہر میں تین فاحش کیا ہو رنین فاحش لڑکی کے بارہ میں تو یہ ہے کہ اُس کے مرشل سے اتنی کمی کردی ہو جتنی کی تو نا گوارا نہیں ہوسکتی اور لڑکے کے بارہ میں یہ ہے کہ عمو اُس کا نکاح جس لڑکی سے ہوا ہے اُس لڑکی کے مہر مثل سے اتنا زیادہ مقرر کیا کہ نے اگر کوئی با اولا د عورت یا مرد مجنوں ہو جاوے تو اُس کا سب سے مقدم ولی بٹیا ہے اور بیٹے کا کیا ہو ا نکاح سب احکام میں اُسی نکاح کے برا ر ہے جو باپ نے کیا ہوا لے یہ حکم جب ہے جبکہ نکاح کرنے کے وقت باپ کو غیر تو ہونے کا علم ہو اور اگر اس نے زوج یا مل زوج وغیرہ کے بیان کی بنارہ پر کفو سمجھ کر کیا تھا اور بعد میں ثابت ہوا کہ کفو نہیں تو اُس کا حکم خیارہ قامت میں معلوم ہو گا
Khiyar al-Bulugh (option of puberty). The foremost guardian of a minor boy and girl is the father. If the father marries a minor, that marriage becomes binding — meaning after puberty, the boy or girl does not have the option to annul it, whether the marriage was to a compatible (kuf') or incompatible, and whether a proper mahr was fixed or there was a gross deficiency in mahr. Gross deficiency for a girl is that her mahr was reduced from her mahr al-mithl to an amount that cannot be tolerated; and for a boy, it is that the marriage [?] with a girl whose mahr al-mithl is such that he fixed an excessive mahr. If a woman with children or a man becomes insane, then his foremost guardian is his son and [؟]. The marriage done by [؟] is the same in all rulings as the marriage done by the father. This ruling applies when at the time of marriage the father knew of non-compatibility. But if he relied on the statement of a spouse or in-law and thought it was compatible, and later it turned out incompatible, then its ruling will be known under khiyar al-qamat (option of deficiency).
Source pages p. 152

Commentary
This passage explains the Hanafi ruling on khiyar al-bulugh (option of puberty). According to Hanafi law, the father (or paternal grandfather) has the authority to marry his minor children. Such a marriage is binding (lazim) upon the minor once they reach puberty; they have no right to annul it. This is regardless of whether the spouse is socially compatible (kuf') or not, and regardless of whether the mahr was fair or grossly deficient. However, there is an exception: if the father was unaware of the incompatibility at the time of marriage because he relied on false information from the spouse or relatives, then the minor may have an option (khiyar al-qamat) upon puberty. The passage also mentions that in cases of insanity, the son becomes the guardian. Overall, this reinforces the father's absolute authority in marriage for minors, with a narrow exception for deception.

Key Terms
خیار بلوغ (khiyar al-bulugh): The option that a minor has upon reaching puberty to annul a marriage that was contracted by someone other than the father or grandfather. Here, the text clarifies that when the father contracts the marriage, this option does not exist.
ولی (wali): Guardian with authority to marry a minor; foremost is the father.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when does a minor have the option to annul a marriage contracted by the father?
A) Always, if the marriage was not to a compatible spouse.
B) Never, the father's marriage is always binding.
C) Only if the father was unaware of the incompatibility and relied on false information.
D) Only if the mahr was grossly deficient.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, the father's marriage for a minor is binding and cannot be annulled upon puberty unless the father was deceived about the spouse's compatibility.

Father's Marriage for Minor: Binding or Void?
Hanafi ruling on khiyar al-bulugh and conditions for binding marriage.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule on khiyar al-bulugh by identifying when a father's marriage for a minor is binding and when it becomes void due to bad judgment or intoxication.

Core Idea
A father's marriage for his minor child is binding only if he is of sound mind and not notoriously imprudent; otherwise, it is void.

In Hanafi family law, a father's authority over his minor children includes the power to marry them off. But this authority is not absolute. The marriage is generally binding — the child loses the option of puberty (khiyar al-bulugh) — provided the father meets two key conditions: he must be of sound mind at the time of the contract, and he must not be known for bad judgment (su' al-ikhtiyar). This lesson unpacks those conditions and their implications for the validity of the marriage.

Guided Path
1
Identify the default ruling
The passage states that when the father marries his minor son or daughter, the marriage is binding (lazim). The child has no option to annul it after puberty, regardless of whether the marriage is to an equal (kufu) or with fair dower.
This is the baseline rule.
2
Note the exceptional circumstances
The passage then introduces two situations that would normally render a marriage problematic: marrying a non-equal (ghayr kufu) or with fraudulent excess in dower (ghubn fahish). For these situations, the marriage is still valid but only if two conditions are met.
Without these conditions, the marriage would be void.
3
Examine Condition 1: Sound mind
The father must be in his full senses at the time of the marriage contract. If he was intoxicated or otherwise mentally incapacitated, the marriage is absolutely void (batil), regardless of other considerations.
Intoxication invalidates the contract entirely.
4
Examine Condition 2: Not known for bad judgment
The father must not be a person who is notoriously known for poor decision-making (ma'ruf bi su' al-ikhtiyar) due to greed or imprudence. The passage also states that an openly sinful (fasiq) person falls under this ruling. If the father is such a person, his marriage with non-kufu or excessive dower is void.
The condition is based on the father's reputation, not a single mistake.
5
Apply to agents and grandfathers
The same ruling applies if the grandfather performs the marriage or if an agent is appointed. An agent given general authority cannot marry the minor to a non-equal or with excessive dower; doing so voids the marriage.
The agent's authority is limited by the father's instructions.

Source Text

خیار بلوغ
ہے
نابالغ لڑکے اور لڑکی کا سب سے مقدم ولی باپ ہی ہے۔ اگر باپ نابالغ کا نکاح کردے تو وہ نکاح لازم ہو جاتا ہے یعنی بلوغ کے بعد بھی لڑکے لڑکی کو اس کے فسخ کرانے کا اختیار نہیں رہتا خواہ کفو میں نکاح کیا ہو یا غیر کفو ہو۔ اور خواہ مہر مثل مقرر ہوا ہو یا مہر میں غبن فاحش کیا ہو۔ غبن فاحش لڑکی کے بارہ میں تو یہ ہے کہ اس کے مہر مثل سے اتنی کمی کردی ہو جتنی کو ناگوار نہیں ہو سکتی اور لڑکے کے بارہ میں یہ ہے کہ عموماً اس کا نکاح جس لڑکی سے ہوا ہے اس لڑکی کے مہر مثل سے اتنا زیادہ مقرر کیا کہ وہ زیادتی عموماً ناگوار سمجھی جاتی ہے۔ مگر غیر کفو کے ساتھ اور غبن فاحش پر نکاح کے صحیح ہونے کے لیے دو شرطیں ہیں۔ اول یہ کہ وہ شخص نکاح کرنے کے وقت ہوش و حواس سالم رکھتا ہو پس اگر نشہ کی حالت میں ایسا کیا تو نکاح بالکل ہی باطل ہے۔ دوسری شرط یہ ہے کہ معروف بسوء الاختیار نہ ہو یعنی اس کے قبل کوئی واقعہ ایسا نہ ہوا ہو جس کی بناء پر عموماً خیال ہو جاوے کہ یہ شخص معاملات میں لالچ وغیرہ کی وجہ سے مصلحت اور انجام بینی کو مد نظر نہیں رکھتا۔ پس اگر کوئی شخص لالچ یا نا عاقبت اندیشی کے سبب بد تدبیری میں مشہور و معروف ہو وہ اگر نابالغ بیٹے یا بیٹی کا نکاح غیر کفو سے کر دے یا مہر میں غبن فاحش کرے تو وہ نکاح بھی بالکل باطل ہے۔ اور جو شخص فاسق یعنی بے باک اور بے غیرت ہو وہ سوء الاختیار کے حکم میں ہے كما فی اوائل باب الولی من الدر المختار مع الشامی (مہم جلد) اس کو خوب یاد رکھیں اکثر لوگ اس سے ناواقف ہیں۔ اور ان دونوں شرطوں کے بعد اگر باپ، دادا خود نکاح پڑھا دیں تب بھی یہی حکم ہے اور اگر مقدار مہر معین کر کے کسی معین شخص سے نکاح پڑھانے کے لیے کسی کو وکیل بنادیا ہے تب بھی یہی حکم ہے لیکن اگر کسی کو مہر کی مقدار اور شوہر کی تعیین کئے بغیر ہی وکیل بنا دیا کہ میری لڑکی کا کسی جگہ نکاح کردو تو اس وکیل کو غیر کفو سے اور غبن فاحش پر نکاح کرنے کا اختیار نہیں اگر کر دیا تو باطل ہے كما يأتی من الدر فی العبارات الآتیة من قوله دوان كان المزوج غيرهما الخ ومن قوله لو حسين لوكيله القدر الذي مر غبن فاحش الخ وكما اذا صرح فی الوكيل ان ارقال زوجها بخین فاحش زوجها بغير كفو
Option of puberty. The foremost guardian of a minor boy or girl is the father. If the father marries the minor, that marriage becomes binding, meaning that after puberty the boy or girl does not have the option to annul it, whether the marriage was to an equal or not, and whether the dower was set as the proper dower or there was fraudulent excess in dower. Fraudulent excess regarding a girl is that the dower is reduced from her proper dower by an amount that cannot be tolerated, and regarding a boy is that the dower is set so much higher than the proper dower of the girl he marries that the increase is generally considered objectionable. But for the validity of marriage to a non-equal and with fraudulent excess, there are two conditions: First, that the person was in sound senses at the time of marriage; if he did so while intoxicated, the marriage is absolutely void. Second, that he is not known for bad judgment, meaning that no prior incident occurred upon which it is generally concluded that this person, due to greed etc., does not consider benefit and foresight in affairs. So if a person is notorious for poor management due to greed or imprudence, and he marries his minor son or daughter to a non-equal or with fraudulent excess in dower, then that marriage is also absolutely void. And a person who is openly sinful, i.e., reckless and without honor, is in the ruling of bad judgment, as in the beginning of the chapter on the guardian from al-Durr al-Mukhtar with al-Shami (important volume). Remember this well; most people are unaware of it. And after these two conditions, if the father or grandfather himself performs the marriage, the same ruling applies; and if he appoints someone as agent to perform the marriage to a specific person with a specified amount of dower, the same ruling applies. But if he appoints an agent without specifying the amount of dower and the husband, saying 'marry my daughter somewhere,' then that agent does not have the authority to marry to a non-equal or with fraudulent excess; if he does so, it is void, as will come from al-Durr in the following expressions: from his saying, 'If the one marrying is other than them...' and from his saying, 'If Husayn says to his agent, the amount that involves fraudulent excess...' and as if he explicitly states to the agent, 'Her marriage with fraudulent excess' or 'marry her to a non-equal.'
Source pages p. 152

Commentary
This passage is a Hanafi ruling on the option of puberty (khiyar al-bulugh) when a father marries his minor child. It clarifies that under Hanafi law, a father's marriage for his minor son or daughter is binding (lazim) and cannot be annulled after puberty, regardless of whether the marriage is to an equal (kufu) or not, and whether the dower is fair or excessive (ghubn fahish). However, the validity of such a marriage depends on two conditions: the father must be of sound mind (not intoxicated) at the time of the contract, and he must not be known for bad judgment (su' al-ikhtiyar) — i.e., he must not be a person who is generally reckless or neglectful of consequences due to greed or imprudence. If either condition is violated, the marriage becomes void (batil) rather than simply voidable. The text also notes that an openly sinful (fasiq) person is considered to have bad judgment for this purpose, citing the Durr al-Mukhtar. It further extends this ruling to cases where the grandfather marries the minor or where an agent is appointed: if the agent is given general authority, he cannot marry the minor to a non-equal or with fraudulent excess; doing so renders the marriage void.

Key Terms
خیار بلوغ (khiyar al-bulugh): The option of the minor after puberty to annul a marriage contract made during minority.
کفو (kufu'): Equality in social status, lineage, wealth, profession, etc., between spouses in marriage.
غبن فاحش (ghubn fahish): Excessive discrepancy between the dower (mahr) and the proper dower (mahr al-mithl) that would be generally considered intolerable.
معروف بسوء الاختیار (ma'ruf bi su' al-ikhtiyar): A person notoriously known for poor decision-making, often due to greed or imprudence, such that his actions are not trusted.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi ruling in this passage, what is the consequence if a father marries his minor daughter to a non-equal while he is intoxicated?
A) The marriage is valid but she can annul after puberty
B) The marriage is absolutely void (batil)
C) The marriage is valid only if she consents after puberty
D) The marriage is suspended until the father becomes sober

Key Takeaway
A father's marriage for his minor child is binding and removes khiyar al-bulugh only if the father is of sound mind and not notoriously imprudent; otherwise, the marriage is void.

Silence and Khiyar al-Bulugh: Virgin vs Minor
Rule statement on khiyar al-bulugh with commentary

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on khiyar al-bulugh: the virgin's option is lost by silence if she knows; the minor's option persists until explicit consent or indication.

Core Idea
Silence invalidates the virgin's option but not the minor's — the minor's khiyar lasts a lifetime until explicit acceptance.

This lesson examines two distinct cases of khiyar in marriage: the option of a virgin given choice, and the option of a minor upon reaching puberty. The Hanafi school draws a sharp line: silence has different legal effects based on who is silent and their knowledge. Understanding this distinction is essential for issuing fatwas in marriage option cases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two parties
Distinguish between the virgin (bikr) who is given choice and knows the marriage, and the minor (saghir) who reaches puberty. Note that the rule also covers an old woman (shaykh) who attains puberty.
The text mentions 'al-saghir wa al-shaykh' — minor and old woman.
2
State the rule for each
For the virgin: her khiyar is invalidated by silence (sukut) if she is aware. For the minor and old woman: silence does not invalidate their khiyar; it continues until explicit consent or a clear indication.
The virgin's rule is an exception to the general principle that silence cannot be taken as consent.
3
Note the time duration
The virgin's silence invalidates the option immediately; it does not extend to the end of the session. The minor's option is not limited by session — its time is a lifetime (waqtuhu al-umr).
The phrase 'la yamtaddu ila akhir al-majlis' indicates the immediacy for the virgin.
4
Recognize indicators of consent
For the minor, explicit acceptance is required. Indicators include a kiss, giving a symbolic coin (fals), or paying the dower (mahr). These are signs of satisfaction that terminate the khiyar.
Even silence in the same session does not count as consent for the minor.
5
Apply to modern fatwa scenarios
Consider a minor girl married by her guardian who reaches puberty and remains silent for years. Her khiyar persists. Conversely, a virgin who is told of the marriage and remains silent immediately loses her option if she understands.
This distinction protects minors from being bound by silence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم قال ( وبطل خيار البكر بالسكوت) لومختارة (عالمتريب) اصل والنکاح) ولا يمتد الى اخر المجلس وان جهلت به بخلاف المعتقة روخيار الصغير والشيب اذا بلغا لا يبطل ) بالسكوت (بلا صريح ) رضاء ( اودلالة) عليه كقبلة فلس و دفع مهر (ولا يبطل ريقيا مهما عن المجلس ) لان وقته العمر فيبقى حتى يوجد الرضاء انتقى مختصر ا و قال الشامي رقوله لو مختارة) امالي بلغها الخبرها العطاس او السعال فلما ذهب عنها قالت له ارضی باز الرد اذا متصلا الم رقوله لا اخر المجلس ) اى مجلس بلوغها او علمها بالنكاح كما في الفتح (قوله وان جعلت به ) ای ها خيار البلوغ او بانه لا يمتد (قوله والشيب) شمل ما لو كانت شيبا فى الاصل ا و كانت بكرا ثم دخل بھا تو بلغت كما في البحر وغيره (قوله دفع مهر) حمله في الفتح ل ينبغي ان التفصيل الاتي في البكر والعيب بعد البلوغ يجرى فى المجنونة بعد الافاقة والله اعلم
Then he said: (And the option of the virgin is invalidated by silence) if she is given choice (knowing the matter and the marriage) and it does not extend to the end of the session, even if she was ignorant of it, unlike the freed slave woman. And the option of the minor and the old woman when they reach puberty is not invalidated by silence without explicit consent or an indication of it, such as a kiss, a symbolic coin, or payment of dower. And it is not invalidated by their remaining in the session, because its time is a lifetime, so it remains until consent appears. End of the abridged version. And al-Shami said: His saying 'if she is given choice' - such as when her news reaches her by sneezing or coughing, then when it leaves her, she says 'I am pleased' or 'I refuse' if it is continuous? His saying 'to the end of the session' i.e., the session of her attaining puberty or her knowledge of the marriage, as in al-Fath. His saying 'and if she is ignorant of it' - i.e., the option of puberty, or that it does not extend. His saying 'and the old woman' includes the case where she was originally an old woman, or was a virgin then entered upon her and then reached puberty, as in al-Bahr and others. His saying 'paying dower' is carried in al-Fath. It is appropriate that the following detail about the virgin and the defect after puberty applies to the mad woman after her recovery. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 159

Commentary
This passage presents two distinct rules: First, the option (khiyar) of a virgin (bikr) is lost by silence if she is given a choice and knows the marriage; it does not extend to the end of the session, unlike a freed slave woman. Second, the option of a minor boy or girl (or an old woman) who has reached puberty is not lost by silence; it continues until there is explicit consent or an indication such as a kiss or paying the dower. The commentary clarifies definitions and extensions, including that the option of a mad woman after recovery follows similar rules. The author emphasizes that the minor's option persists over a lifetime until acceptance is expressed.

Key Terms
بكر (bikr): A virgin woman who has not been previously married.
سكوت (sukut): Silence that indicates acceptance or waiver of option, depending on context.
خيار البلوغ (khiyar al-bulugh): The option to annul a marriage upon reaching puberty, for minors married by non-ascendants.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
How does the option of a minor who reaches puberty differ from that of a virgin?
A) It is lost by silence
B) It persists until explicit consent or indication
C) It lasts only until the end of the session
D) It requires a judicial decree to be valid

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi rule: the virgin's khiyar al-bulugh is lost by silence if she knows; the minor's khiyar persists indefinitely until explicit acceptance.

Khiyar al-Bulugh: The Option of Puberty in Hanafi Marriage Law
Explaining khiyar al-bulugh rules in marriage contracts by non-guardians

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi ruling on khiyar al-bulugh to identify when a minor spouse retains the option to annul a marriage arranged by a non-guardian.

Core Idea
A minor married by a non-guardian retains the option to annul upon puberty; adult virgins lose it by silence.

Welcome to this lesson on khiyar al-bulugh (the option of puberty). In Hanafi fiqh, when a minor boy or girl is given in marriage by someone other than their father, grandfather, or son, they have the right to annul the marriage once they reach puberty. This lesson, based on a passage from al-Shami's commentary, explains the conditions under which this option is retained or lost, with special attention to the effect of silence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and the marriage contract
Determine who conducted the marriage (the guardian). The option only applies if the marriage was by a non-guardian (e.g., uncle, brother, not father/grandfather/son).
The text specifies 'other than father, grandfather, and son'.
2
Recognize the trigger for the option
The option arises at the moment of puberty (bulugh) or, if puberty occurred without knowledge of the marriage, upon learning of it. The minor must be aware of the marriage to lose the option by action.
The text mentions 'by puberty or knowledge of it'.
3
Distinguish the effect of silence
For a minor (saghir/saghirah), silence after reaching puberty does NOT void the option. For an adult virgin who is aware and free to choose, silence after knowledge DOES void the option. This is a critical distinction.
The text states: 'the choice of the virgin is void by silence if she is choosing and aware... the choice of the minor and the old woman is not void by silence'.
4
Understand the duration of the option
The option for minors is not limited to a single session; it lasts throughout life ('its time is lifetime') until explicit consent or a clear indication of acceptance (e.g., accepting dower after entry) is given.
The commentary emphasizes that the option persists 'until consent appears'.
5
Apply the exceptions and special cases
Consider cases like an insane woman who recovers, dower payment after entry, and whether entry occurred before puberty. These affect whether an action counts as consent.
Al-Shami discusses the insane woman analogously to the minor and treats dower payment after entry as possible consent.

Source Text

And similarly, it is said about her acceptance of dower after entry or seclusion, as taught in Durr, vol. 2.
Source pages p. 159

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (from al-Shami and others) explaining the rulings on khiyar al-bulugh (option of puberty) in Hanafi fiqh. When a minor (male or female) is married by someone other than the father, grandfather, or son, the minor has the right to annul the marriage upon reaching puberty or upon learning of the marriage. The passage details various cases: if the minor is a virgin, her silence after knowledge of the marriage may void her option if she is aware and choosing; but for minors (both male and female) who have not expressed consent, silence does not void the option—it remains until explicit consent or a clear indication (like accepting dower) is given. The option is not limited by the session; it persists throughout life until exercised. The commentary also addresses special cases such as an insane woman who recovers, and the effect of dower payment or acceptance after entry. The key distinction is between the option of a virgin adult (which can be lost by silence) and that of a minor (which cannot be lost by silence alone).

Key Terms
خيار البلوغ (khiyar al-bulugh): The right of a minor who was married by a non-guardian to annul the marriage upon reaching puberty
السكوت (al-sukut): Silence after knowledge of the marriage; for minors it does not void the option, but for adult virgins it may
الصغير والصغيرة (al-saghir wa al-saghirah): Minor male and female who have not yet reached puberty

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) When she is a minor reaching puberty

D) When she is a freed slave

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, a minor married by a non-guardian retains the option of puberty until explicit consent; silence does not forfeit this right, contrary to the rule for adult virgins.

The Father's Marriage of a Minor to Non-Kufu and Tacit Consent
Ruling on father marrying minor children to non-kufu via tacit consent

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that a father may marry his minor child to a non-kufu and identify acts that constitute tacit consent, thereby preventing annulment.

Core Idea
A father's marriage of his minor child to a non-kufu is valid; tacit actions after puberty confirm the marriage.

This lesson examines the sixth case from Ashraf Ali Thanwi's Al-Hilat al-Najijah, where a father marries his minor child to a non-kufu. The ruling hinges on tacit consent (dilalat al-rida) — certain acts by the child after puberty indicate acceptance, barring any later challenge. This is a crucial legal tool (hila) for ensuring stability in marriages where kufu may be an issue.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
Recognize that the father (or grandfather) has the authority to marry his minor child to a non-kufu. The marriage is valid subject to the child's option at puberty.
This is based on the father's wilayat al-ijbar.
2
List actions of tacit consent
According to the text, actions like the husband touching with desire, kissing, paying nafaqah, or the wife accepting mahr or allowing intimacy indicate tacit consent.
These actions are evidence that the child confirms the marriage after puberty.
3
Note the condition on mahr acceptance
Acceptance of mahr is evidence of consent only if no valid khalwa (khalwa sahiha) has occurred after puberty.
If khalwa has occurred, the acceptance is not needed as consent is assumed.
4
Extend the rule to guardians
The son of an insane or mentally deficient person (majnun/ma'nun) takes the place of the father in these rulings.
This is a qiyas: the son has wilayat when the father is incapacitated.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
نکاح نابالغ و نا بالغہ چھٹی صورت چھٹی صورت یہ ہے کہ نابالغ لڑکے یالڑکی کا نکاح اُس کے والد یا دادا نے ایسے شخص سے کیا جس کو جیسے کہ شوہر ، عورت کو شہوت سے چھوتے یا شہوت سے عورت کا بوسہ لے یا ہریا نان و نفقہ ادا کرے اور بیوی اس کو بوسہ دے یا شہوت سے چھونے پر قدرت دے دے یا مهر وغیرہ قبول کرلے تو یہ دلالتہ رضامندی ہے اور مہر کا قبول کرنا رضامندی کی دلیل اُس وقت ہے جبکہ بالغ ہونے سے خلوت صحیحہ نہ ہو چکی ہو جیسا کہ درمختار میں اور رد المتخار میں صراحت ہے۔

کے اسی طریقہ سے مجنون اور معنون کا بیٹا ان احکام میں باپ کے برابر ہے جسے کہ گذر چکا نکاح میں کفر ہونے کی شرط : نکاح میں اگر کفار کی نہ تو رکی تھی لیدی می بیند ونی)
The sixth case: The marriage of a minor boy or girl by his father or grandfather to such a person... For example, if the husband touches the wife with desire, or kisses her with desire, or pays nafaqah, and the wife gives him a kiss or allows him to touch with desire, or accepts the mahr, then this is tacit consent. And acceptance of mahr is evidence of consent when no valid khalwa (seclusion) has occurred after reaching puberty. As explicitly stated in Durr al-Mukhtar and Radd al-Muhtar. Similarly, the son of an insane or mentally deficient person [؟] is like the father in these rulings. As passed: condition of kufu in marriage, if [؟].
Source pages p. 168

Commentary
This passage outlines the sixth case: the marriage of minor children by their father or grandfather to a non-kufu person. The text focuses on how tacit consent (dilalat al-rida) from the original wali is established. In Hanafi law, the father or grandfather has the authority (wilayat) to marry off minors, and such a marriage is valid even if the spouse is not kufu (since the father's authority is comprehensive). However, if the child reaches puberty and becomes baligh, they have the option to annul if the marriage is against kufu? But the text here discusses how actions of the spouse (e.g., touching with desire, kissing, paying nafaqah) or the acceptance of mahr by the wife (or her wali) constitute tacit consent to the marriage, precluding any later objection. This is similar to the rule for a thayyib. The text then makes an analogy: the son of an insane or mentally deficient person (majnun or ma'nun) takes the place of the father in these rulings, meaning he may exercise wilayat over his minor children if the father is incapacitated. The final part appears to restate the condition of kufu. The author relies on Durr al-Mukhtar and Radd al-Muhtar as sources. This case is a classic hila for ensuring a valid marriage despite kufu concerns, by conditioning it on later consent or by the father's authority.

Key Terms
دلالۃ رضامندی (dilalat al-rida): Tacit consent inferred from actions (e.g., accepting mahr, allowing intimacy)
خلوت صحیحہ (khalwa sahiha): Valid seclusion between spouses that implies consummation

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, acceptance of mahr is evidence of tacit consent only if which of the following has NOT occurred after puberty?
A) Khalwa sahiha (valid seclusion)
B) Payment of mahr
C) Touching with desire
D) Visiting parents

Key Takeaway
Father's marriage of minor to non-kufu is valid, but tacit consent by the child after puberty removes the right to annul.
